This report is based on the findings of a mission which visited Vietnam in
June/July 1996. Task Team members included Mmes./Messrs. Anjum Altaf (Team
Leader), Carlos Escudero (Sr. Counsel), Jack Fritz (Environmental Engineer),
Maninder Gill (Resettlement Specialist), Hiroyuki Hayashi (Research Assistant),
Hilary Kiell (Procurement Analyst), Jaroslav Kozel (Sanitary Engineer), John
Nicholson (Financial Analyst), Thanh Nguyen Cong (Sanitary Engineer), and Manida
Unkulvasapaul (Environmental Specialist) . Peer reviewers were Messrs. Edouard
Motte (Senior Sanitary Engineer, EA2EM) and Raja Iyer (Ptincipal Management
Specialist, EA3IN) . Peer advisers were Mmes./Messrs. Penelope Brook Cowen (PSD),
Alexander Bakalian (LAlIU) and Robert Roche (AFTUl). The report was edited by Ms.
Barbara Koeppel and task support was provided by Ms. Martha Ochieng. The Division
Chief is Mr. J. Shivakumar (EAIIN) and the Director is Mr. Javad Khalilzadeh-
Shirazi (EAlDR).



VIETNAM
WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
I. BACKGROUND
A. Population and Development
1.1         Vietnam is the second most populous country in Southeast Asia with a
1995 population of 74 million projected to reach 80 million by 2000. About 15
million (20%) of the population is urban. Four major cities Ho Chi Minh City, Hanoi,
Danang and Haiphong account for 37% of the urban population with the remainder
living in about 500 locations. About 80% of the urban population is concentrated in
77 cities with population over 15,000.
1.2         Literacy (over 90t for adult males and females) and life expectancy (68
years) are above the less developed country (LDC) averages while access to safe
water supply (50% urban, 25% rural) is below the LDC average. Lack of safe water and
adequate sanitation facilities are the major causes of Vietnam's higher incidence
of water-borne and hygiene-related diseases such as gastroenteritis, dysentery,
typhoid, cholera and hepatitis which also contribute to childhood morbidity and
mortality. The ten leading causes of morbidity and mortality are practically all due
to communicable diseases, most of them preventable.
1.3         Economic growth has accelerated with the introduction of reforms in
1986. Since then it has averaged around 7-8 percent per annum leading to increased
urbanization (estimated to grow by 70% in next ten years) and rising urban incomes.
Foreign investment has been attracted to Vietnam but limited by infrastructure
constraints in urban areas. Infrastructure in most cities and towns, laid at the end
of the 19th and beginning of the 20th centuries, has not been adequately maintained
or upgraded because of resource constraints.
1.4         The Government has responded to the above by adopting in 1991 the Socio-
Economic Stabilization and Development Strategy to the Year 2000. The aspects of the
strategy relevant to the proposed project are to:
(i)   focus limited resources available to develop infrastructure to promote
investment in services and industries in three priority areas: (a) Hanoi-Haiphong-
Quang Ninh; (b) Ho Chi Minh City-Bien Hoa-Vung Tau; and (c) the Danang area;
(ii) streamline the number of line ministries, focus policy-making within the
central government and decentralize implementation to local governments; and
(iii) improve the management mechanism of state enterprises, experiment and
gradually expand the transformation of state enterprises into private companies.
B. Water Supply and Sanitation
1.5         Water supply and sanitation infrastructure has been affected by extreme
resource constraints in the past, by lack of access to quality materials due to
isolation of the Vietnamese economy, by the socio-political necessity for state
enterprises to spread resources thinly in providing basic services free or for
minimum fee to as many as possible, and by the acceptance of minimal service
standards by consumers during times of national adversity. Given the conditions, the
performance of the Water Supply Companies (WSCs) has been quite remarkable.



2
1.6         Piped water is available to about 50% of the urban population with
access ranging between 60%-80% in large cities and under 30% in other towns.
However, material quality, cumulative deferred maintenance over decades, and
inadequate expansion have taken their toll. Pumping and treatment facilities are
overloaded and inefficient with ageing and non-compatible equipment leading to unit
power consumption double international averages while distribution systems leak
excessively. As a result, service is intermittent with low pressure, per capita
water availability is low (below 120 liters per capita per day - lcd) and 80% of the
water supplied does not meet national water quality standards. In addition, WSCs
have been oriented towards using subsidies to meet social targets with minimal
incentive to recover costs from user charges. Therefore, billing and collection
functions have been neglected. Physical water loss and inadequate billing and
collection have combined to yield non-revenue water (NRW)1 of over 50% in some WSCs.
1.7         Urban sanitation facilities have suffered similarly. Sewerage coverage,
at 60% of population, is higher in Ho Chi Minh City (HCMC) but low in other cities,
including Hanoi (30%) . The collection network is in need of extensive repair and
treatment of wastewater is non-existent leading to pollution of inner city waterways
and groundwater. Solid waste collection in city centers is well organized but
disposal suffers from lack of proper landfills. No facilities exist for disposal of
hazardous and industrial waste.
C. Sectoral Issues
1.8         The objective of rapidly overcoming the overwhelming quantitative and
qualitative deficiencies resulting from prolonged under-investment defines the key
sectoral problems. These pertain to mobilization of the resources required;
formulation of a sector strategy to most effectively utilize available resources and
to increase resource mobilization in the future; selection of a sector structure
best suited to achieving sector objectives; organization of enterprise management
to ensure financial sustainability of investments; and control of construction
quality to ensure physical sustainability of the assets provided. Second order
issues resulting from the pursuit of rapid sector development pertain to the
protection of vulnerable social groups and prevention of negative environmental
impacts.
1.9         Concerning mobilization of resources for the sector,  it has to be
recognized that at present the aggregate requirement of all infrastructure sectors
exceeds available resources resulting in allocations for particular sectors that are
insufficient to meet total demand. While private capital is flowing to Vietnam,
returns in a number of sectors are presently higher than those available in the
water supply and sanitation sector. Thus prioritization in the use of available
allocated resources and efforts to increase the attractiveness to private capital
are important sectoral issues.
1.10        The transition to a market economy in Vietnam gives rise to especially
challenging sectoral concerns. Sector policies, strategy, structure and organization
1 Non-revenue water (NRW) refers to the water produced that does not bring in revenue, inter alia, due to
losses in the network (leaks), pilferage, non-billing and inaccurate metering.



3
need to be compatible with an environment changing from a supply to a demand
orientation. Presently the most significant steps required pertain to the re-
orientation of enterprise management to providing water and sanitation services as
a business rather than exclusively as a social obligation, to the gradual
replacement of subsidies by user charges, and to the increased ability of
enterprises to make rational business decisions without unnecessary political
control. At the same time, because of the natural monopoly characteristic of the
sector, the role of state agencies needs to evolve into that of regulators rather
than providers of service (para 4.1).
1.11        Issues arising from the existence of a sector monopoly in a low-income,
market economy need to be anticipated. These pertain to consumer and environmental
protection and ensuring universal access in the case of an essential commodity like
water. Adequate regulation and tariff design are needed to take care of consumer
concerns while project and program planning are needed to cater for environmental
consequences of increased water supplies (para 5.3).
D. Government Strateqy for the Sector
1.12        Both supply and demand side considerations consequent  to economic
reforms have led the Government to focus attention on the water sector. Inadequate
infrastructure could undermine plans to attract foreign investment and tourism while
increased urbanization could overwhelm existing facilities and lead to social
dissatisfaction. Proposed environmental legislation requires improvements in water
quality and rising incomes and higher user charges increase consumer expectations
for better service levels. Thus, resource allocation for both quantitative and
qualitative investments is required.
1.13        Based on a UNDP/World Bank Water Supply and Sanitation Sector Study
(1990)  and a Finnish-funded National Urban Water Supply Strategy  (1994),  the
Government has formulated a two-stage sector development strategy. The first stage
focuses on system rehabilitation and renovation to meet demand up to the year 2000
and on institutional strengthening of the WSCs to ensure that the physical
investments are sustainable. The second stage would address demand and capacity
expansion beyond the year 2000.
1.14        Physical targets in the strategy's first stage call for development in
53 provincial towns to serve 70-80o of their population. Estimated investments to
attain these goals would be of the order of US$ 1 billion. This far exceeds the
sectoral allocation of the Government. Since, at this stage, the country risks
remain too high, individual project size relatively small, and financial returns
inadequate to attract substantial private sector investment, the Government has
decided to request financing for the key cities targeted for priority development
(para 1.4) from the Asian Development Bank (ADB) for HCMC and IDA for Hanoi,
Haiphong, Danang and Quang Ninh; for the other towns it decided to use bilateral
low-cost or grant financing contributions as much as available.
1.15        The accompanying institutional strengthening aims to ensure that the
physical investments are sustained and operated efficiently. Elements of the
strengthening being coordinated with the State Enterprise Reform Program include
decentralization of responsibility to the provincial level; increased autonomy for
WSCs with elimination of overlapping jurisdictions; re-orientation of WSCs on a more



4
commercial foundation; increased cost recovery through user charges and gradual
elimination of central subsidies. Considerable external development support has also
been directed at institutional strengthening of national sector agencies to improve
capabilities to plan and prepare sector strategies and projects, to develop policy
guidelines, standards and procurement and construction guidelines, and to strengthen
project management, implementation and monitoring capacity.
1.16        The Government strategy does include involvement of the private sector
through Build-Operate-Transfer (BOT) projects for bulk water provision mostly for
industrial estates where commercial tariffs are presently considered affordable.
However, water provision to urban customers is planned to remain the responsibility
of reformed State Enterprises at least in the first stage to the year 2000.
E. IDA Strategy and Rationale for Involvement
1.17        The IDA approach formulated in the Country Assistance Strategy2 is in
agreement with the Government's two-stage sector development plan which is based on
the UNDP/World Bank Sector Study and the National Urban Water Supply Strategy. The
CAS identifies poor infrastructure as a key bottleneck to development, transition
to a market economy as a prime objective and reform of State Enterprises as an
important goal. The CAS states that the Bank would take the lead among donors to
help the Government plan and finance infrastructure. Commercialization of the WSCs
would also indirectly support the goal of poverty alleviation by redirecting
existing subsidies to WSCs for other activities with higher social returns.
1.18        The IDA strategy places greater emphasis on institutional strengthening
of the WSCs to ensure that the investments are sustained and operated efficiently
and that the sector becomes attractive for private sector participation. However,
it is clear that institutional improvements alone are not sufficient to meet the
targets for the year 2000. In particular, water services cannot be improved without
increasing the capacity and operational efficiency of treatment plants and physical
leakages cannot be reduced without major replacement of obsolete tertiary pipelines.
In addition, improved revenue collection requires investment in metering of
household connections. Thus the IDA strategy is based on a judicious mix of
essential physical investments supported by the institutional development necessary
to sustain the physical investments. Available documentation confirms that the ADB
is also following a similar strategy for its investment in HCM City.
2 Memorandum of the President (Report No. 15053-VN) dated October 25, 1995 and the Progress Report
discussed by the Board in conjunction with the proposed credit for a Rural Transport project (IDA/R96-186) on
December 23, 1996.



5
II. SERVICES IN THE PROJECT AREA
A. The Project Area
2.1         Based on the Government's regional priorities (para 1.4), the project
would include investments in Hanoi, Haiphong, Danang and Halong City/Cam Pha (Quang
Ninh province). Hanoi-Haiphong-Quang Ninh form the northern economic triangle and
Danang is the focal point for development in central Vietnam. Danang (close to the
historic city of Hue) and Halong City (located on Halong Bay, a UNESCO World
Heritage site) are also marked for development as tourist attractions. Hanoi,
Haiphong and Danang are cities with provincial status.
B. Population
2.2         Hanoi is the national capital and second largest municipality after HCM
City. Urban population was 1.1 million in 1995 and is expected to reach 1.5 million
by 2010. The urban area is projected to grow from 50 km2 in 1990 to 83 km2 in 2010.
Haiphong is the main seaport in northern Vietnam and the fourth largest
municipality. Urban population was 430,000 in 1995 and is projected to reach 600,000
by 2010. The urban area is 20 km2. Danang, is the major deep-water seaport in
central Vietnam with good access to communications networks through National Highway
#1 and the Trans-Vietnam railway. It is the third largest municipality in the
country. The 1995 urban population of 450,000 is expected to reach 600,000 by 2010.
2.3         Quang Ninh province, the country's principal coal exporter, is situated
about 160 km northeast of Hanoi. It is small province with a population of 900,000,
of which about 36% (320,000) are urban. The two urban communities, Halong City and
Cam Pha, are both important ports. Halong City is the provincial capital; it's
western section (Bai Chai) is developing a tourist center separated from the rest
of the city by the mouth of the Dien Vong River. Cam Pha lies along the coast to
the northeast, about 20 km from Halong City. The combined population of the two
cities is expected to grow from 278,000 in 1995 to 500,000 in 2010, mainly by
absorbing neighboring districts into an urban agglomeration.
C. Water Supply
2.4         Water supply services are provided to domestic, commercial, industrial
and institutional consumers by provincial WSCs responsible to the provincial
People's Committees (PCs) . In 1995, the Hanoi Water Business Company (WBC) served
900,000 residents (82% of the urban population) and other consumers through 123,000
connections and 1000 standposts. Average production was 320,000 m3/d of which 69%
was NRW.   Estimated domestic consumption was 120 lcd. The Haiphong WSC served
420,000 residents (98% of the urban population) and the others through 56,000
connections and 650 standposts. Average production was 125,000 m3/d of which about
69% was NRW. The Danang WSC served 191,000 residents (47% of the urban population)
and the others through 21,000 connections. Average production was 52,000 m3/d, of
which 33% was NRW. The Quang Ninh WSC served 43,200 residents (15% of the urban
population) and the others through 13,000 connections.   Average production was
28,000 m3/d of which 56% was NRW.
2.5         Of the total connections, 25% are metered in Hanoi, 56% in Haiphong,
100% in Danang and 50% in Quang Ninh. Numerous meters are out of order and many



6
commercial establishments are registered as domestic consumers qualifying for a
lower tariff. Non-metered domestic consumers are billed a fixed monthly charge based
on an estimated consumption of 3-6 m3 per capita per month. Non-metered commercial
and industrial consumers are charged based on negotiated contracts related to
estimated monthly usage. Both situations lead to excessive water use, sharing of
connections between families, incentives for meter tampering and leakage of revenue
in contracts based on estimation of monthly usage.
2.6         With some exceptions in Hanoi, the condition of water supply facilities
is poor and treatment facilities are overloaded. Existing water demand cannot be
met, large segments of population must use private, unhygienic wells where
groundwater is accessible, and the quality of piped water is below national
standards. Tertiary distribution networks have not been developed in any of the
cities. They have been replaced by extremely long individual connections (spaghetti
connections)  that draw water directly from mains.   However,  these were never
pressurized over one bar; also, external and internal corrosion is common, and
joints and fittings are leaking, generally beyond repair (see Annex 1 for a detailed
description of water supply, sewerage and solid waste services).
D. Sewerage. Drainage and Solid Waste
2.7         Sewerage service is provided by the Sewerage and Drainage Companies
(SDCs) in Hanoi and Haiphong and Urban Environment Companies (URENCOs) in Danang,
Halong City and Cam Pha.  In addition to sewerage and drainage, URENCOs manage
street cleaning, solid waste collection and disposal, and maintain public toilets,
parks and street lights. The SDCs and URENCOs are State Enterprises but unlike WSCs
are responsible to municipal and not to provincial PCs.
2.8         The existing drains are combined sewers' serving city centers. They are
over 50 years old, built during the French period. Hanoi SDC maintains 120 km of
sewers and 35 km of open channels serving about 30% of the urban area; the Haiphong
SDC maintains 170 km serving three urban districts; the Danang URENCO maintains 37
km serving about 30% of the urban area and the Halong and Cam Pha URENCOs maintain
about 53 km. The systems are overloaded and in poor condition, often flood severely
for extended periods, and discharge wastewater without treatment. The population
without access to the sewer systems uses on-site sanitation, mostly septic tanks
without adequate drain fields. Only about 35%-40% of the solid waste generated is
collected. The rest is burned or buried at the source or scattered. The existing
landfills do not comply with environmental and health requirements, are mostly
filled, and will need to be closed soon.
E. Project Demand and Rationale
2.9         In 1991, the Government asked the IDA to help prepare a water supply and
sanitation project to remedy the above-mentioned deficiencies in the four cities.
The objective of the water supply component was to meet coverage and consumption
' Combined sewers provide the service in one pipe for surface water drainage as well as wastewater, which
must first pass through septic tanks (or suitable pre-treatment) to remove solids and toxins before entering the sewer.
Separate sewers provide a separate pipe for each service.



7
targets for the year 2000, improve water quality, rehabilitate facilities, reduce
physical leakages and to improve institutional capacity of the WSCs. The objective
of the sanitation component was to rehabilitate drainage and sewerage networks,
improve flood protection, improve solid waste management, provide new sanitary
landfills, and strengthen operating agencies. During preparation, however, it was
found that the great number of involved institutions with various financial
objectives would make the project very complex (para 3.4) . It was decided,
therefore, to reformulate the initial project into a water supply project to be
processed first and a sanitation project to follow later.
2.10        Investments  in the water  supply  sector are needed now.  Deferred
investments have had a negative effect on national development plans and the quality
of urban life and further delays would increase the final cost of rehabilitation.
Historic funding levels have been low. During 1983-86, urban water supply
expenditures were less than 1.5% of the Government's total capital investments,
while provincial contributions to water supply projects accounted for 5-7% of total
provincial budgets. Allocations increased to 6% of total central Government
investment by 1989, but decreased in real terms, from US$ 8.3 million in 1983 to US$
0.4 million in 1989. Including provincial capital allocations, this corresponded to
an overall investment volume of US$ 0.56 million in 1989. Similarly, capital
investment levels in sewerage and waste removal in the three major cities was less
than US$ 1.5 million by 19922.
2.11        While the low funding partially reflects the low absorptive capacity of
the sector, the constraints have been gradually removed. Progressive reforms have
delegated more responsibilities to the provinces and increased the autonomy and
responsibilities of the WSCs including the ability to borrow for capital expansion.
At the same time, the provinces have allowed the WSCs to retain funds that were
earlier paid back to the province (e.g., depreciation charges) in order to augment
their ability to self-finance their operations. Tariffs have also been increased
significantly in real terms and subsidies have been curtailed to move the WSCs
towards a more customer and efficiency oriented mode of operation. While
institutional reforms need to continue, they have progressed to the stage where
project investments in the WSCs are feasible.
2.12        There  are  no  viable  project  alternatives.  From  a  development
perspective, the investment forms an integral part of the overall development of the
three key economic regions identified by the Government. From a financing
perspective, Government resources are insufficient and private sector investment is
not forthcoming at present without tariff increases that would be politically and
socially unsustainable3.  From an engineering perspective, complete replacement of
the run-down systems would be prohibitively expensive and presently unaffordable.
Use of development assistance for judicious rehabilitation of existing assets,
2 Historical investments in the sector are reported in "Business Opportunities in Vietnam in the Water
Supply and Sanitation Sector," GKW Consult, 1994. The report is available in the project file.
3 The long run marginal cost of water in Hanoi is presently estimated to be more than double the existing
average water tariff.



8
essential capacity expansion and institutional change to increase operational
efficiency remains the least cost and most affordable alternative.
F. Related Projects and Lessons Learnt
2.13        As a result of low national funding allocations and Vietnam's economic
isolation, international counterpart financing in the water sector has been slow.
Assistance to the sector since 1985 has not exceeded US$ 100 million. The most
significant projects which can yield useful lessons have been financed by.Finnida.
The first project in Hanoi is now in its third and final phase with cumulative
investment up to 1995 of US$80 million. The second project started in Haiphong in
1990 and cumulative investment has been approximately US$20 million.
2.14        Valuable lessons can be derived from a comparison of the experiences of
the two projects. The Hanoi project was started in 1985 before sector reforms were
initiated in Vietnam and, as an emergency, was managed by a contractor focusing on
physical investments. While significant physical investments have been made in the
network and water quality has improved, WBC performance and customer service levels
have not shown commensurate improvement. After 10 years, NRW remains above 60%.
Lessons from the Hanoi experience have been utilized in the design of the Haiphong
project with better results. There has been a greater emphasis on initial
engineering preparation and institutional development; the community-based model
chosen for system rehabilitation at the neighborhood level has reduced NRW from over
60% to below 40% in the rehabilitated areas.
2.15        However,  further explanation is needed for the differences  since
institutional development efforts initiated in Hanoi since 1990 have not yielded
similar results. Part of the reason lies in the nature of the physical systems in
the two cities. Hanoi, with easy access to groundwater, has never generated
sufficient pressure on the PC or WBC management to change established modes of
operation. By contrast, Haiphong with no access to groundwater was faced with acute
water scarcity in the summer of 1993 with rioting directed against the WSC resulting
in the death of a company employee. As a result, the PC changed the WSC management,
and gave a clear mandate to the new Director to show results. The combination of
accountability from above and pressure from below provided the driving force for
improved performance. These lessons are being incorporated in the project by
institutionalizing the two driving forces through annual performance agreements
between the PCs and WSCs and public performance audits to increase consumer
involvement (paras 4.19 & 4.21).
2.16        Analysis of the Danang WSC confirms the above observations.  Acute
scarcity of water owing to very limited treatment capacity has forced PC involvement
and WSC efficiency in operations. As a result 100% of the connections are metered,
disconnection policies are strictly enforced, and billing and collection monitored.
As a result NRW is about 33% and performance bonuses from retained earnings yield
employee salaries double those in Hanoi. This confirms the assessment that
significant improvements can be made to the distribution system and the methods
employed for metering, billing and collection, often without major capital
expenditure. Therefore, these components are an essential part of the project and
the basis for projecting NRW to decrease from over 60% in some WSCs to about 40% by
the time steady state conditions are achieved.



9
2.17        The Hanoi and Haiphong experiences also suggest lessons for the design
of institutional and technical assistance components. While this remains a
contentious issue, appraisal assessment is that the strategy of seconding long-term
advisors as management counterparts without time-bound, task-specific assignments
subject to periodic evaluation is not the most cost effective. Lack of a credible
option of withdrawing advisors if essential recommendations are not implemented
undermines project effectiveness and is ultimately counterproductive. Technical
assistance components in the project will be focused on specific, monitorable tasks
to be completed within specified time periods. Inclusion of Hanoi in the project was
made conditional on the introduction of a credible program to reduce NRW. The PC
responded with a concrete measure, signing a contract with an international private
water operator to initiate customer management in one supply district. The first
phase of the contract is due to be completed in March 1997 and the current results
are promising.
2.18        While the project would be the first IDA involvement in the sector and
first project to deal with subnational entities, lessons based on the Bank's global
experience are incorporated in the project design. The principal lesson is that
sector projects should not be used as vehicles to pursue subsidiary objectives at
the expense of the main objective. Thus tariffs should not be distorted to satisfy
poverty alleviation objectives; instead, direct subsidies should be provided to
needy households, if warranted. Similarly, water supply projects should not be used
to develop the construction industry if such development affects construction
quality. Dividing procurement packages into small contracts with the simplest sub-
components (e.g., house and meter connections) reserved for the smallest and least
experienced contractors has yielded an extremely expensive lesson. Poor construction
of such sub-components results in over 95k of physical leakages which are hard to
detect and expensive to repair because the assets are underground. The procurement
arrangements for the project have been designed to eliminate such problems.
2.19        Cautionary lessons have been learnt from the experience of the ADB
financed project in HCM City. The project which was signed in 1994 was suspended in
December 1996 because of slow implementation and disbursement. The project included
33 procurement packages each of which had to be evaluated and separately approved
by the Prime Minister. As the first multilateral funded projects at the provincial
level in a changing sector environment, it is to be expected that unanticipated new
issues would arise during project preparation and implementation that would take
time and patience to resolve.
2.20        Another key lesson from the Bank's global experience is to avoid trying
to do to much in one project and to refrain from attempting policy or procedural
changes that cut across sectors. The project design is not over-ambitious and
recognizes that all sector objectives cannot be achieved in one project because of
the constraints imposed by the realities on the ground (first time borrower, first
project dealing directly with State Enterprises at the sub-national level, a
transition economy in which sector policies are not firmly established, provincial
and enterprise management relatively unfamiliar with the requirements of market-
based operations, low staff salaries, consumer affordability considerations, limited
counterpart funds, limited debt service capability and limited absorptive capacity).
Therefore, the project has been designed as part of a careful, phased strategy
structured around an investment program comprising in the four cities at least two
separate water supply and sanitation projects each. The first water supply project



10
includes measures that are most critical and yield the greatest immediate benefits
while setting in motion policy reforms and actions that would ensure the successful
attainment of all objectives by the completion of the investment program. However,
reform of generic problems cutting across sectors such as unsystematic loan
processing procedures or cumbersome funds flow arrangements at the country level are
not and cannot be addressed through the project.



11
III. THE PROJECT
A. Project Oriqin and History
3.1         The Government's  goal with respect  to urban water supply and
sanitation is to improve the quality of drinking water and waste water, to
develop infrastructure to meet defined targets and to commercialize the operating
companies. To achieve these objectives, it has sought external financing. In
1991, it requested IDA funding for a water supply and sanitation project in four
major cities and secured bilateral assistance to prepare the project from Finland
(for Hanoi and Haiphong), Denmark (for Quang Ninh) and Australia (for Danang).
3.2         Project processing was held up till 1993 because of delays in signing
agreements between the Government and bilateral donors. Project reviews in 1993
identified project complexity as a major risk since water supply, sewerage and
sanitation services are provided by different companies with different funding
arrangements. However, it was decided to proceed with the project design on the
grounds that the Ministry of Construction (MOC), responsible for urban water
supply and sanitation at the national level, would coordinate the project
components. As part of project preparation, technical assistance was arranged
from Australian aid to strengthen the regulatory capacity of the MOC.
3.3         However,  in  1995,  further  to  the  decentralization  of  sector
responsibilities to the provincial level, the Government decided that MOC would
remain actively responsible for managing projects only in secondary cities. Major
cities, which included those in the IDA-assisted project, would be directly
responsible for their projects. While institutionally this constituted a step in
the right direction, freeing the WSCs from parallel chains of command,
operationally it resulted in the project design becoming unmanageable.
3.4         As a result, it was agreed to divide the initial project into two,
a water supply project, to be processed first and a sanitation project to follow
later, and to outline a sector investment program for the four cities as follows:
(a) Water Supply Project to be implemented in 1997 to rehabilitate existing
facilities and meet demand up to 2000; (b) Sanitation Project to be implemented
in 1999 to restore functionality of existing sewerage and drainage facilities,
to provide septic tank maintenance program and to improve solid waste management
including construction of sanitary landfills; (c) Second Water Supply Project to
be implemented from 2000 to expand capacity to meet demand up to 2010; and (d)
Second Sanitation Project to be implemented in 2002 to expand wastewater
collection and treatment to handle the increased water production resulting from
the Second Water Supply Project. The sanitation projects would exclude sewerage
and drainage in Hanoi which is being funded by OECF of Japan and possibly solid
waste management. Both the Second Water Supply Project and the Second Sanitation
Project would be prepared within the respective first projects.
B. Objectives and Design
3.5         The aims of the first water supply project in the four cities are to:
(a) improve the quality of water supply services in Hanoi, Haiphong, Quang Ninh
and Danang through renovation of existing facilities in order to satisfy demand
up to 2000 of about 2.5 million people in the cities; (b) ensure sustainability



12
of the physical investments by developing the institutional capabilities of water
supply agencies, facilitating their commercialization, and upgrading their staff
skills through training; and (c) assist in preparation of a future investment
program to satisfy water demand after 2000.
3.6         At the present time, a project based on concessional financing,
maximum rehabilitation of existing facilities, limited capacity expansion and
concentrated institutional improvements is the dominant, least cost alternative.
Government resources are insufficient and private investment is not forthcoming
in distribution of essential services because of unfavorable risk/return
characteristics. Large scale replacement of existing facilities is unaffordable
since tariff increases to meet full cost recovery are only possible in a
graduated manner because of social and political implications.
3.7         To ensure that the selection of engineering sub-components conformed
to the specific needs and preferences of the individual cities, the following
mechanism was adopted. Based on an initial assessment of the financial capacity
of the WSCs, an investment envelope was allocated to the WSC. The WSCs, in
collaboration with engineering consultants, identified alternative sub-components
to meet given project objectives (coverage, consumption and quality targets)
which were assigned weights depending upon the specific situation of each city.
Sub-components were ranked based on their overall impact measured in terms of
expected contribution to each target weighted by the importance of the target.
The rankings, made independently by consultants and WSCs, were discussed in a
meeting attended by members of the provincial administration, and the highest
ranked sub-components within the investment envelope were included in the
project. The remaining sub-components were deferred for consideration in the
follow-up project. This procedure ensured that the selection of sub-components
was made in a participatory manner and that the project is optimally sized, not
too large to be financially unsustainable by the WSCs and not too small to fall
short of essential targets.
C. Description
3.8         The project would include the following elements:  (a) renewal and
upgrading of water supply facilities in the urban districts of Hanoi, Haiphong
and Danang, and Halong/Cam Pha in Quang Ninh province; (b) institutional measures
to ensure sustainability of the physical investments; (c) support activities
consisting of various studies, advisory services, supervision of construction and
training; and (d) resettling and rehabilitating the population affected by the
project. The project locations are shown on maps IDA Nos 28592 through 28595 and
a detailed description is provided in Annex 2.
3.9         The renewal and upgrading of water supply facilities would include
the following:
In Hanoi, (a) production wells and two treatment plants; (b) primary, secondary
and tertiary distribution networks, including large water meters; and (c) new
water connections, replacement of existing connections made of galvanized steel
(GS) pipe and provision of water meters.
In Haiphong, (a) a raw water pumping station and raw water transmission main; (b)



13
a treatment plant; (c) primary and secondary distribution pipelines, including
a balancing reservoir; (d) a tertiary distribution network in 17 wards (phuongs)
with water connections and provision of water meters; and (e) equipment,
materials and vehicles for operations and maintenance.
In Danang, (a) a water intake, transmission main and access road; (b) a treatment
plant and reservoir; (c) a treated water transmission main, primary, secondary
and tertiary distribution network with connections and provision of water meters;
and (d) equipment, materials and vehicles for operations and maintenance.
In Quang Ninh, (a) deep wells; (b) raw water transmission and raw water pumping
stations; (c) two treatment plants; (d) transmission and primary distribution
mains; (e) a secondary and tertiary distribution network with connections and
provision of water meters, booster pumping stations and water reservoirs; and (f)
equipment, materials and vehicles for operations and maintenance.
3.10        Institutional measures in WSCs would include:  (a) upgrading the
accounts receivable systems; (b) improving customer management (mapping,
registration, disconnections-reconnections, etc.); and (c) preparation of O&M
guidelines for each WSC as a basis for establishing adequate budget allocations
which is an essential condition for upgrading of operation and maintenance
procedures to sustainable levels; (d) corporate planning models.
3.11        Support activities would include construction management and various
studies concerning (a) the quality of surface water sources, evaluating monitored
data, and identifying actions aimed at protecting the sources against pollution;
(b) identifying, quantifying, monitoring and evaluating NRW; (c) periodic audit
of the safety of Cao Van dam in Quang Ninh (para 3.33) and introducing corrective
actions, and (d) staff training and professional evaluation of managers and
operators in collaboration with international private water system operators.
TORs for institutional and support activities and various studies are listed in
Annex 13 (1-09, 2-09, 3-09, 4-16) and available in the project file.
3.12        The follow-up water supply investment and institutional development
program would expand water production and distribution capacity to meet system
demands beyond 2000 and position the WSCs for self-financing and private sector
participation. TORs for the engineering components should be completed by the
WSCs in March 1999. TORs for the institutional components would be formulated
following the mid-term review of the first water supply project.
D. Status of Preparation
3.13        The WSCs, assisted by consultants engaged under bilateral grants,
have completed preliminary or detailed engineering designs'; pre-qualification
and bidding documents for Hanoi, Haiphong and Quang Ninh were completed in April
1997 and for Danang will be completed in July 1997.   The designs follow
international standards for the water supply sector and have been technically
reviewed by Bank staff and found satisfactory. Pre-qualification and bidding
documents follow the Bank's standard documents.
1 Excluding details of distribution networks.



14
3.14        On the institutional side, technical assistance at the national level
for the MOC consisting of capacity building for project design and management,
preparation of design and construction guidelines and implementation of training
curricula carried out under a separate Australian assistance has been completed.
A pilot program to improve customer management has been started in Hanoi WBC by
Saur International (SI), a reputed private water system operator. Also, billing
and collection procedures in Hanoi WBC have been evaluated by an accounting firm,
Coopers and Lybrand. The firm was employed under a PHRD grant in January 1997 to
improve the procedures and the accounts receivable system in Haiphong.  As.a part
of institutional effort and commercialization of WSCs a workshop to be organized
by the EDI is planned for August 1997 to transfer the Bank's global experience
in performance agreements, public performance audits (para 4.21) and corporate
modeling to key policy and decision makers and related academics in Vietnam.
3.15        Environmental impact assessments (EIAs) and environmental management
plans (EMPs) for the four cities were prepared as part of preliminary designs.
Resettlement action plans (RAPs) for Hanoi, Haiphong, Quang Ninh and Danang were
prepared as part of detailed designs before appraisal (para 3.27) . The EIAs for
the components were cleared and approved by the Ministry of Science, Technology,
and Environment (MOSTE) in August and October 1996. The EMPs were reviewed during
appraisal and are satisfactory.
E. Cost Estimates
3.16        The project cost is estimated at VND 1569.21 billion (US$142.66
million), with a foreign currency requirement estimated at VND 812.98 billion
(US$73.91 million) or 52% of the total.   Base costs reflect June 1996 price
levels. Physical contingencies are estimated at US$5.89 million or 5% of base
costs; price contingencies are at US$18.94 million, or about 16% of base costs
plus physical contingencies, and assume annual rates of inflation for local costs
of 7% for 1997, 6% for 1998-1999 and 5% for 2000-2004.   For foreign costs,
inflation is estimated at 2.3% from 1996 onwards. Cost estimates were based on
preliminary designs of the Quang Ninh and Danang components and completed
detailed designs of the Hanoi and Haiphong components.
3.17        The total cost of implementation support is estimated at US$15.85
million, which represents about 3,350 person-months, 2,250 of which would be for
supervision of construction and 1,100 for various studies and training.  The
input of national consultants is estimated at 2,700 person-months while
expatriate input is estimated at 650 person-months. Cost estimates are listed
in Annex 3 and summarized in Table 3.1.

F. Financing Plan
3.18        The proposed IDA credit would be funded under the Interim Trust Fund
(ITF) administered by IDA and subject to special provisions on procurement. The
credit of SDR 71.3 million (US$98.61 million equivalent) would finance 69% of
project costs net of duties and taxes. Finland would finance US$3.89 million for
studies, advisors and training in Hanoi and Haiphong, Denmark would finance
US$4.7 million for studies, advisors, training and construction supervision,
US$3.06 million for installation of individual connections2 and US$5.0 million
for construction of water supply facilities3 in Quang Ninh, and Australia would
finance US$1.33 million for studies,  advisors and training in Danang.   The
remaining US$26.07 million would be financed locally. The plan is summarized in
Table 3.2.

3.19        The borrower would be the Socialist Republic of Vietnam.  The 40-year
ITF credit would include 10 years of grace at standard IDA terms. The ITF credit,
Danish grant to GOV and Danish Trust Fund for Quang Ninh would be onlent to water
companies by the Government in VND on the following terms: The fixed interest
rate would be 6.5% per annum, the loan would be repayable over 20 years with a
5 year grace period. The Government would bear the exchange rate risk, and
interest during the grace period would be waived. The onlending terms take into
account the fact that the WSCs need a subsidy at this initial stage of their
restructuring. The waiver of the interest during construction is a special
contribution of the Government to the WSCs. Separate project agreements have been
executed between IDA and the WSCs with covenants on operational, financial and
monitoring aspects.
3.20        At negotiations the onlending terms were confirmed and assurances
obtained from the Government that it would provide through PCs local funds as
required by WSCs for carrying out their parts of the project. The execution of
the Australian, Danish and Finnish Grant Agreements, and Danish Trust Fund would
be a condition of effectiveness of the ITF credit. Execution of subsidiary loan
agreements between the Government and the WSCs would be a condition of
disbursement of the ITF credit for the respective project component.
G. Implementation
3.21        The WSC in each province would employ contractors to supply equipment
and construct the proposed facilities. In Hanoi, Haiphong and Danang, they would
also hire engineering firms to act as the construction management consultants
(CMC), while in Quang Ninh, the CMC would be provided through Danish aid. The
CMCs would report to WSC directors and for daily activities, the directors would
delegate responsibility to the component coordinators, who would head the
component implementation units (CIUs) within the WSCs. Together, the WSCs and



17
provincial governments have established component implementation committees
(CICs) to help the WSCs implement the works by facilitating administrative
actions. The CMCs would also serve as secretariats to the CICs and report on
implementation progress at regular meetings (see Chart 1).
3.22        Construction is expected to start in October 1997 and be completed
by June 2002 (para. 3.41) . Project works are expected to be operating in all WSCs
by the end of 2001, but the installation of water connections and other minor
works may extend for an additional six months.   The schedule  (Annex 4) is
considered realistic since each component involves just one contract for both
civil works and the supply and installation of goods. Supervision would be aided
by firms of consultants. Employment of construction management consultants
acceptable to IDA would be a condition of disbursement for the related part of
the project.
3.23        Institutional and other studies and training would be financed by
Finnish, Danish and Australian grants that would be provided to Vietnam under
bilateral agreements. The program of implementation support, training and outline
TOR for studies agreed upon with co-financing agencies are available in the
project file. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that beginning in 1997
WSCs would submit annual plans for implementation support and training to IDA
each year by October 30.
3.24        The WSCs have prepared project implementation plans that define the
scope and timing of actions needed to carry out the project. An understanding was
reached at negotiations that each WSC would carry out its project implementation
plan. The plans are available in the project file and summarized in Annex 5.
H. Land Acquisition and Resettlement
3.25        Water production facilities and transmission mains would require
acquisition of about 45 ha of land, of which 9 ha would be for temporary use and
36 ha for permanent use, involving in total 1,772 households. The Hanoi and
Haiphong components would not involve relocation of affected persons. In Hanoi,
12 ha would need to be acquired for permanent and 3 ha for temporary use
involving in total 1,362 households. In Haiphong, 1.5 ha would need to be
acquired for permanent use involving 44 households. The Quang Ninh component
would require 8 ha of land, of which 4 ha would be for temporary use, and 4 ha
for permanent use. Of 465 various structures located on top of water mains, some
would be demolished or relocated to ensure the pipelines are safe and to protect
water quality. This would involve 212 households in total. The Danang component
would require 20 ha of land of which 2 ha would be for temporary use, and 18 ha
for permanent use. About 13 structures would be partly demolished and remodelled
on their plots. Land acquisition would involve 154 households of which 13 would
have structures affected.
3.26        Collection of data on distribution pipelines prior to appraisal was
not feasible since their exact location will be known only at implementation.
Thus, in respect of distribution components, RAPs with data on specific
resettlement activities would be completed when all the information on the land
to be acquired and its owners are available, and submitted to IDA for approval
prior to start of works. All distribution pipelines would be under public roads.



18
It is estimated that approximately 150 ha of land would need to be acquired for
temporary use. The policy framework for these (distribution) RAPs would be the
same as outlined in the RAPs for transmission components (paras 3.27 and 3.28).
3.27        Following  OD 4.30  on  Involuntary Resettlement,  WSCs  submitted
satisfactory resettlement action plans (RAPs) which include a policy framework,
detailed Action Plans for the production and transmission components of the
project and provision for preparing Action Plans for the distribution components.
The RAPs were approved by the respective PCs and by the Government of Vietnam.
The Prime Minister's approval includes special decisions releasing the
enforcement of Vietnamese regulations related to replacement rates for valuation
of assets and to non-compensation to unauthorized settlers on acquired land.
3.28        Implementation of detailed RAPs would be regularly supervised and
monitored by WSCs and reported to the respective Component Implementation
Committee and PC. The status would also be included in semi-annual progress
reports submitted to IDA. In addition, an external agency, acceptable to IDA,
would be employed by WSCs to semi-annually carry out monitoring and evaluation
of RAP implementation and to prepare a final report on completion of each RAP.
Assurances were obtained at negotiations that the WSCs would implement their
respective RAPs and would arrange for: (i) semiannual progress reports on WSCs'
supervision and monitoring of resettlement activities; (b) starting from December
31, 1997, an independent semi-annual monitoring of RAPs' implementation; and
(iii) final report on completion of each RAP. IDA approval of a detailed
(distribution) RAP in respect of each distribution network site would be required
prior to commencement of works on that part of the project. These would be
prepared by WSCs and approved by PCs. The establishment of Resettlement
Committees by each of the PCs pursuant to the provisions of the RAPs would be a
condition of effectiveness of the ITF credit.
3.29        The Quang Ninh component would draw water from the reservoir created
by the Cao Van dam. The filling of the reservoir in 1996 involved the
resettlement of 117 families  (688 individuals)  belonging to the Do ethnic
minority. The Government started the resettlement in early 1992 by meeting with
affected households and by December 1996 the resettlement was completed. The
households selected the areas where they wanted to settle: 69 households
resettled in Gia Lai province in the Central Highlands while 48 households
resettled within Quang Ninh province. In addition to provision of land for land,
compensation at replacement rates was paid for houses, trees, crops and other
assets lost, along with transport to the new area. Civic infrastructure, such
as roads and schools, was provided in the new areas.
3.30        A resettlement specialist on indigenous people reviewed the program's
status in October 1996. He concluded that: (a) the Cao Van construction planning
and design included resettlement costs; (b) its organization followed government
guidelines and decisions, with budgetary allocations and funds disbursed
annually; (c) the affected population was recognized as a special ethnic group;
(d) resettlement locations were chosen by the project affected people (PAP) and
the move was implemented in several phases, accommodating expressed preferences
and customs; and (e) the resettled households have legal access to productive
resources that allow for sustainable, but relatively intensified annual cropping
patterns. The report is available in the project file. In March 1997, a field



19
visit was made to Gia Lai province where 69 affected households have relocated.
A number of issues pertaining to this resettlement are considered outstanding.
These include completion of facilities for and asset transfer to households
resettled in Gia Lai province (5.3 ha of land, electrical connections to 25
families and dry season irrigation facility) and information on living standards
of 12 families who self-relocated in Quang Ninh province. Necessary actions
pertaining to these were agreed at negotiations and covenanted in the Project
Agreement. Compliance with the obligation to provide the assets (land and
electrical connections) to households resettled in Gia Lai province would be a
condition of disbursement of the proceeds of the credit earmarked to finance all
of Quang Ninh WSC project components. A dated covenant, June 30, 1998, was
included in the Project Agreement with regard to completion of the irrigation
facility in Gia Lai province.
I. Safety of Cao Van dam
3.31        The Cao Van dam was designed,  constructed and financed by the
Government. The dam is an earthfill structure 28 m high and 225 m long creating
a reservoir with a water volume of about 11 million m3. Construction of the dam
started in 1992 and was essentially completed in early 1996. Minor works such as
clearing, planting of grass and some reforestation will extend to 1997. The
purpose of the reservoir is to immediately provide about 60,000 m3/d of raw water
for Halong water supply system which will be increased to 120,000 m3/d after the
dam is raised in about 2010.
3.32        On IDA request the Quang Ninh WSC, assisted by Danish aid, arranged
in 1995 for an independent dam specialist4 to (a) inspect and evaluate the
safety status of the Cao Van dam, and (b) provide recommendations on any safety-
related remedial measures5. In his finding, the specialist stated that the
construction was carried out to a good standard and no safety problems should
occur when the dam is completed and recommended that the design flood and the
hydraulic design of the spillway be further studied. In addition, the report
suggested that (a) routine safety inspections of the dam should be carried out
by the safety engineer of Quang Ninh WSC twice a year, and (b) independent safety
inspections by an independent specialist be carried out annually. The inspection
report is available in the project file.
3.33        The Quang Ninh  WSC  has  agreed  to  implement  the  specialist's
recommendations and the Danish aid program includes the carrying out of the
recommended studies and assistance with periodical safety inspections (para
3.11) . TOR for the studies are available in the project file. At negotiations
assurances were obtained that the Quang Ninh WSC would furnish by December 31,
1997 proposed arrangements for periodic inspections of the Cao Van dam and that
it would implement recommendations of such inspections thereafter.
4 Mr. David Morris, Member of "All Reservoirs" Panel set up in accordance with Reservoirs Act 1975
under United Kingdom legislation.
I Inspections of dams which are not part of the project but necessary for its performance is required by the
OP 4.37, Safety of Dams (September 1996).



20
J. Procurement
3.34        Procurement would be carried out according to the Guidelines for IBRD
Loans and IDA Credits, dated January 1995, revised in January and August 1996 6.
The four major supply and installation contracts that comprise the bulk of
procurement (US$111.33 million) would involve supplying and installing mechanical
and electrical equipment. These contracts would be procured through international
competitive bidding (ICB) following the pre-qualification of bidders and using
the Bank's Standard Bidding Documents (SBDs), Standard Pre-qualification Document
and Standard Bid Evaluation Form. ICB procedures and SBDs would also be used to
procure most of the vehicles and equipment (US$2.09 million) . Small equipment for
maintaining the network, workshops and laboratories, and office equipment
packaged in individual contracts that do not exceed US$100, 000 each or US$890, 000
altogether, would be procured by shopping7. These would consist of small value
contracts awarded by the four WSCs. For procurement under ICB procedures,
domestic goods, plant and equipment would be allowed a preference margin of 15%
or the amount of customs duties, whichever is lower, for bid evaluation. A
general procurement notice was published on March 31, 1996. In addition, specific
procurement notices will be advertised for all contracts exceeding $10 million
for goods and works and $200,000 for consultants. No mandatory associations
between foreign and local consultants will be permitted. Further general
procurement notices will be issued for outstanding project components including
expected consultancy assignments. Prequalification notices were published in
Development Business. Procurement arrangements are detailed in Annex 4 and
summarized in Table 3.3.
6 Notwithstanding the provision of paragraph 1.6 of the Guidelines, the proceeds of the Interim Fund Credit
may only be withdrawn on account of expenditures for goods, works and services provided by nationals of, and
produced in or supplied from ITF Participating Countries.
7 For international shopping, a minimum of three quotations from at least two countries will be solicited.

to achieve a sampling ratio of 1 in 10. All contracts with consulting firms,

K. Disbursement
3.37        The proceeds of the ITF credit and Danish fund would be disbursed
against: (a) 85t of total expenditures of supply and installation contracts; (b)
100% of foreign expenditures for imported equipment and materials; (c) 100% of
local expenditures (ex-factory) for domestically manufactured equipment and
materials, or 65% of local expenditures for other equipment and materials
procured locally and (d) 100% of total expenditures for consultant services.
3.38        Disbursement for equipment and material contracts of US$100,000 or
less, for services provided by consulting firms under contrcts of US$100,000 or
less, and for services provided by individual consultants under contracts of
US$50,000 or less would be made on the basis of a statement of expenditures
(SOE). Supporting documents for these expenditures would be retained by the
Government, made available for review by IDA supervision missions, and regularly
validated by independent auditors (para 5.10). To facilitate disbursements from
the credit, the WSCs may each open and maintain an ITF special deposit account
(Account) in a commercial bank authorized for this purpose, on terms and
conditions satisfactory to IDA, including appropriate protection against set-off,
seizure or attachment.
3.39        Each of the Accounts would have an authorized allocation of US$1.0
million to be withdrawn from the credit account and deposited in the Account,
provided, however, that authorized allocation shall be limited to an amount
equivalent to US$500,000 until disbursement and outstanding commitments against
the credit allocated to each component equal or exceed the equivalent of SDR
700,000, unless IDA would otherwise agree. Applications to replenish the Account,
supported by documentation, would be submitted regularly (not less than
quarterly) or when the amount withdrawn equaled 30% of the initial deposit.
3.40        Full disbursement of the proceeds of the Danish Trust Fund will be
a condition of disbursement of the ITF credit earmarked for the respective
project component in Quang Ninh Province.
3.41        Project implementation would extend over four and a half years.
Project works are expected to be operating in all WSCs by the end of 2001, but
the installation of water connections and other minor works may extend for
additional six months, to June 30, 2002. The loan closing date would be December
31, 2002. The estimated disbursement schedule (Annex 6) is based on the detailed
project implementation schedule which reflects simplified implementation through
one supply-and-install contract in each project component and advanced
procurement with completed pre-qualification at the time of Board presentation.
As there is no Bank disbursement profile available for water supply projects in



23
Vietnam, the comparison shows the Bank Standard Disbursement Profile (1995) for
water supply and sanitation projects in the East Asia and Pacific Region.
L. Environmental Impact
3.42         Implementation of the project would be beneficial for the living
standards and health conditions of the local population. Rehabilitation of the
existing facilities would reduce NRW while the expansion of treatment plants and
transmission and distribution lines would improve the quality and volume of
water, enhance service levels and raise revenues.
3.43        Negative impacts would include the acquisition of cultivated land,
demolition of structures and inconvenience for the residents during construction.
To mitigate these effects, the project would include environmental monitoring,
which would continue during implementation.   Land compensation would be paid
according to the RAPs. Other compensations would be made to government agencies
(where their property is affected) during the construction of water mains and
distribution networks.
3.44        Temporary  and  manageable  impacts  during  site  clearing  and
construction of the facilities would include increased levels of traffic, dust,
and noise; disruption of water supply; blocked drainage and water pollution due
to soil erosion at excavation sites; and disposal of surplus soil and
construction materials. The impacts on residential areas would be minor since
most of the WTPs, intakes, and other facilities would be situated away from
residential areas. The mountainous area in Quang Ninh where the raw water main
would be laid and the hill in Danang where the WTP would be constructed are
degraded areas and serious impacts on the local ecology are not expected.
3.45        In close consultation with the residents, the pipeline would be laid
along road shoulders where the impacts on households and businesses would be
minimal. During the design phase of the water mains and distribution pipelines,
efforts were made to minimize the impacts on burial grounds, houses, and
buildings, the need to obtain land and to excavate large quantities of soil.
3.46        The contractors would be required to carry out the planning and
construction according to contractual environmental specifications, including (a)
consultations with residents, (b) provision of safe temporary paths for residents
and businesses, (c) safety measures for the public and workers, (d) measures to
reduce erosion, dust, and noise levels due to construction, (e) limited transport
and construction along the main roads in urban areas during rush hours and in
residential  areas at night,  (f)  appropriate  disposal  of surplus  soil and
construction materials, and (g) provision of adequate water by alternate means
when piped water is interrupted.
3.47        Once operating, the WTPs would generate treatment sludge which would
be disposed of in retention lagoons in WTPs and, when dried, would be disposed
of in controlled landfills. Significant net increase of water supply is not
anticipated in the first project. The increased system capacity would displace
water from alternative sources being used by residents not presently served by
the piped system. Significant capacity expansion is planned for the second water
supply project. By that time, the first sanitation project currently being



24
prepared in the four cities (para 3.4) would have been implemented.
3.48        Potential impacts on land subsidence in Hanoi, dam safety in Quang
Ninh, salinity intrusion in Danang, and contamination of water quality at the
intakes and in the water supply systems in all project components have been
identified and will be addressed by the project's implementation support. These
include (a) monitoring of groundwater and land subsidence in Hanoi; (b) programs
to protect water resources and quality for each project component; (c) a safety
program at the Cao Van dam and (d) a program to ensure availability of water
resources in Danang.   TORs for these studies are in the project file and a
detailed description of the environmental impact including involuntary
resettlement is presented in Annex 7.
3.49        WSCs are willing to implement the project with due attention to
protection of environment through mitigating actions specified in the EMPs (para
3.15) and to comply with all the requirements issued by MOSTE within EIA
clearances. Compliance would be monitored by WSCs with the help of consultants
and periodically by the DOSTE in each province. For their part, residents would
participate throughout various stages of the planning and implementation of the
EMPs and the RAPs. Each household would be fully informed by the WSC, district
and communal peoples, committees.   The public relations program would be
continuous. At negotiations, WSCs confirmed that they would implement mitigating
actions specified in their individual EMPs.
M. Supervision and Performance Indicators
3.50        IDA's project supervision would require about 79 staff-weeks over the
life of the project, of which 20 staff-weeks would be at headquarters to review
progress reports, procurement actions and correspondence, 34 staff-weeks would
be in the field and 25 staff-weeks would be at the Bank's Resident Mission in
Vietnam (RMV) . Supervision by headquarters staff (sanitary engineers, financial
analysts and specialists in environmental issues) would focus on institutional
and physical progress while the involvement of RMV staff would center on
coordinating the project's four components. The borrower's contribution to
supervision would entail coordination of IDA visits and reports on various
subjects, such as regular reporting on implementation progress, evaluation
reports on project implementation, including plans for future operations of
respective components of the project.
3.51        Due to absence of a coordinating agency from early stages of project
preparation, the four WSCs started mutual consultations among themselves and, due
to advantageous location, the Hanoi WBC gradually assumed many of the
coordinating tasks. The WSCs therefore agreed that the Hanoi WBC would be
responsible for overall coordination of the project's four components. This would
include communications with the Government, administrative duties in connection
with visits of IDA missions in Vietnam, ensuring timely reporting on progress of
project components, etc. Managing the continuation of on-going consultations
among the WSCs on various technical and financial aspects related to project
implementation company management would be an important function. To facilitate
and expedite communication with IDA, which involves two languages, the Hanoi WBC
would be assisted by a sanitary engineer experienced in Bank procedures and



25
located in the RMV9. An understanding was reached at negotiations that overall
coordination of the project would be the responsibility of Hanoi WBC. A project
supervision plan is presented in Annex 8.
3.52        In addition  to input and output  indicators  used  for project
supervision, annual Performance Agreements between the Provincial Governments and
the WSCs linked to staff compensation are proposed to be used for evaluating WSC
performance and to strengthen management incentives. Also, Public Performance
Audits are proposed to be used to make comparative WSC performance information
available to the public to make consumer voice and benchmarking competition an
effective tool for improving performance. Impact, input and output indicators are
shown in Annex 11 (Table A 11.1). Based on the Performance/Risk Summary (Table
A 11.2), key performance indicators relevant for the project implementation phase
were identified (para 5.41) . These are presented in Table A 11.3 and were
confirmed at negotiations along with the contents of the Borrower's reports, as
outlined in Annex 8. A mid-term review of the project focusing primarily on the
institutional components would take place in June 1999.
9 The National Country Officer in Vietnam of the UNDP/WB Water and Sanitation Program is available
to provide part-time assistance for project coordination.
