IV. THE INSTITUTIONAL ANALYSIS
A. Sector Structure
4.1         The Ministry of Construction  (MOC)  is the main national agency
responsible for water supply and sanitation. Through its departments and
enterprises, the MOC prepares national plans for the sector, regulates various
technical aspects of water and sanitation facilities, approves designs of major
projects, and assists in the implementation of water supply and sanitation
projects. In addition, it has wide responsibilities in areas of construction,
construction supervision and investment.
4.2         As part of decentralization, the responsibility for operating public
utilities, including water supply, was assigned to provinces with elimination of
MOC's direct involvement. PCs created Water Supply Companies (WSCs) as legal
entities1 to be responsible for construction, operation and maintenance of urban
water supply. The PCs have considerable influence on WSCs development policies,
including increased market orientation, setting targets for production and
distribution of water, deciding on WSCs financial policies, including the blend
of tariffs and subsidies. The organization structure of a typical Provincial
Government is shown in Chart 2. At negotiations assurances were obtained that the
Government would cause the PCs to take all actions to enable the respective WSCs
to implement the project and comply with their obligations.
4.3         Water supply services in the project cities are managed and operated
by WSCs which report to the respective PCs. The organization structure of a
typical WSC is shown in Chart 3.
4.4         The optimal sector structure for provision of urban water supply
services in Vietnam has been evaluated under an ADB financed technical
assistance. A number of alternatives including a national water supply company
and municipal companies were assessed but the existing structure based on
provincial WSCs was considered to offer the best potential for sector
development. The appraisal mission concurs with this recommendation. The sector
structure for provision of sewerage and sanitatiLon services would be reviewed in
detail as part of the preparation of the sanitation project.
B. Project Implementation
4.5         The project would be implemented in each province by the provincial
water company: the Hanoi Water Business Company (WBC), the Haiphong Water Supply
Company, the Quang Ninh Water Supply Company and the Danang Water Supply Company.
To aid implementation the WSCs have established Component Implementation
Committees (CICs) with the participation of the provincial PC. CICs are headed
by the Deputy Director of the Transport and Urban Public Works Service (TUPWS)
of the PC responsible for water supply in Hanoi, Haiphong and Danang and by the
Director of the Construction Department (CD) of the PC in Quang Ninh. CICs,
1 Details of establishing WSCs as legal entities with separate legal personality are included in the project's
legal documents.



27
supported by CMCs (para 3.21), would help the WSCs implement the works by
facilitating processing of various administrative actions within the PC including
budgetary planning and financial support. The CIC of each component would also
help the WSC evaluate various institutional and other studies, assist in
implementing proposed institutional improvements within the WSCIs organization,
and advise on content of annual plans for implementation support and training
activities to be provided through Finnish, Danish and Australian technical
assistance (para 3.23) . The implementing arrangements are shown schematically in
Chart 1. An understanding was reached at negotiations that the CIC would be
maintained for the duration of the project.
C. Staffing and Training
4.6         In 1995, the number of employees was 1,720 in Hanoi, 627 in Haiphong,
476 in Quang Ninh and 274 in Danang. Employees per 1,000 connections varied from
about 11 in Haiphong and Danang to 14 in Hanoi and 35 in Quang Ninh. Primarily
due to the projected rise in the number of connections, the ratio is expected to
stabilize below 10 by 2000 in all four cities which is acceptable and within
parameters of major Asian cities2.
4.7         Staff salaries conform to the general scales set by the Ministry of
Labor, but bonuses are also paid for achieving certain financial targets; thus,
income levels are affected by company performance. For example, since Hanoi 's WBC
has operated at a loss in recent years, salaries have been considerably lower
than in Danang and Haiphong, and top-quality employees have been hard to retain.
Those that stay often have to find additional work to meet their family needs.
4.8         Training levels also vary among the four companies, mainly due to the
resources available and management interest. In Hanoi and Haiphong, training has
been supported by the on-going Finnish Program. During this time, 188 staff from
the Hanoi WBC participated in overseas training or study tours, although some
left the company later, and in Haiphong, 17 staff participated in overseas study
tours. In September 1995, Haiphong WSC entered into a twinning-training agreement
with the water company in Turku, Finland and the Asian Institute of Technology
will train 11 Hanoi WBCs managers during 1996. In Quang Ninh and Danang,
resources have not been available for management training.
4.9         All  four WSCs  companies  recruit  technicians  such  as  welders,
electricians and plumbers who are formally trained in national or provincial
vocational schools. However, those working specifically in water trades (such
as pump operators, pipe fitters and meter readers, etc.) are trained in-house.
The training is provided by company engineers and technicians using course
material prepared by the company and normally results in examinations and
improved salary grades of trainees. However, a shortage of funds for training,
lack of training aids such as equipment and tools, as well as standard curricula
and evaluation methods adversely affects the quality and output of training.
4.10        The analysis of training needs to meet the long-term manpower
2 Water Utilities Data Book, Asian and Pacific Region; ADB, November 1993.



28
requirements of the sector has not yet been made, mainly due to the perceived
view in the Government that decentralization of the training to WSCs is the
correct approach and that the training of operating staff without providing means
for physical improvement of degraded facilities has low value. While the idea has
some merit, it is recognized that a study of human resource development for the
sector, including policies to resolve various related issues such as national
licensing of sector trade categories, salary scales, performance incentives, etc.
should be initiated in the near future.
4.11        Experience suggests that the lead time required for carrying out such
a study would exceed the time required to bring the physical components of the
project on line. Project-related training, therefore, to upgrade skills of most
critical trade categories in shortest time was found the most feasible solution
at this time. Greater regional exposure for managers and specialized training in
commercially oriented systems is also proposed. Along these lines a training
needs assessment has been completed for all WSCs and a training proposal has
being prepared in collaboration with the private water operator Saur
International (SI) (para 3.14).
D. Institutional Issues
4.12        Key institutional issues pertain to facilitating the transition of
the WSCs from subsidized entities operating in a planned environment and
providing a minimum level of social service to commercial companies in a market
economy accountable to owners and consumers and self-financing operations and
capital expansion through user charges. Although, as part of the State Enterprise
Reform, the WSCs are expected to recover all operating and maintenance
expenditures by 2000 and contribute to capital expenditures subsequently, the
organizational structure, operating rules and management style required to
support these changes have been slow to evolve. Thus, the WSCs are quite some
distance from their goals of financial independence and customer orientation.
However, the commitment of the provincial PCs and the motivation of the WSC
management to be ready for the transition to the market economy leaves no doubt
that the changes are achievable.
4.13        The principal  changes needed by the WSCs  relate  to increased
operational autonomy, customer orientation, commercial procedures, financial
planning and the institutionalization of accountability and performance enhancing
incentive systems. Implementation of the changes is modulated by the very real
and immediate need to increase revenues quickly. Increased revenues are required
to appropriately fund operations and maintenance, compensate staff adequately and
service the debt for capital expenditures. Increased revenues are also needed to
eliminate subsidies and gradually transform the WSCs into entities operating on
commercial basis.
4.14        In 1995, the Hanoi PC made a credible commitment to reducing NRW by
entering into a technical assistance contract with SI to implement a customer
management program in one district of Hanoi. The first phase of the contract
would be completed in March 1997. A connections survey and inventory of 4328
customers was completed in September 1996. The survey showed that of the 4328
customers, 845 were unidentified in the WSC records, 114 were illegal, and 632
were not receiving any water. This survey indicates the potential for increasing



29
revenues through commercial customer management.
4.15        A decentralized management model based on fully metered zones
operated with community participation is being progressively implemented in
Haiphong since 1995 with good results. NRW in the improved zones has fallen from
over 60% to below 40%. The entire system will be converted to the new model under
the IDA financed project. The Hanoi WBC has agreed on a NRW reduction program
with the Hanoi PC and is implementing it using a similar approach. Comparable
results have been reported in the pilot zones.
4.16        In parallel to the above, accounting consultants Coopers and Lybrand
conducted an evaluation of the billing and collection procedures and accounts
receivable system of the Hanoi WBC in 1995 and made recommendations for
improvement. Based on the study, a Japanese Grant has been obtained for the
Haiphong WSC to improve billing and collection procedures and the account
receivables system. Coopers and Lybrand was selected for the Haiphong contract
and commenced work in January 1997. Similar work would be initiated in Quang Ninh
and Danang with bilateral assistance. Once the accounting systems are
computerized and fully converted to the new national accounting standards,
corporate planning and financial models are proposed to be introduced to
facilitate planning for capital investment programs.
4.17        In Hanoi and Haiphong, pilot NRW detection and evaluation programs
are included in the technical assistance. Based on the asset inventory and
revaluation completed by all WSCs at the end of 1995, operation and maintenance
guidelines based on standard engineering codes of practice are to be prepared to
ensure appropriate budget allocations for the purpose.
4.18        The above initiatives designed to generate the surplus required to
underpin the sustainability of the physical investments would be complemented by
training needed to manage, operate and maintain the facilities effectively in a
market economy. A training needs assessment has been completed for each WSC and
a proposal for a pilot training and evaluation program for the Hanoi WBC has been
prepared in collaboration with SI. It is proposed that based on the evaluation
of the pilot program, the training center in Hanoi be raised to the level of a
national center for training of staff from other WSCs. Training needs of the WSCs
would be identified and evaluated on an annual basis and financed from bilateral
assistance.
4.19        Measures to increase WSC operational autonomy while making the
companies more accountable to the PCs would be based on the introduction of
annual performance agreements between the PCs and the WSCs. Such agreements have
been introduced in Thailand with Bank assistance as part of public sector reform.
Given the natural monopoly in water distribution and the fact that significant
private sector involvement is not yet possible, this constitutes a feasible
option to improve public sector performance. At present, embryonic agreements
related to reduction of NRW exist in Hanoi and a few other WSCs.
4.20        The introduction of other targets in performance agreements between
the PCs and WSCs would integrate project monitoring and evaluation into the
management incentive system in a dynamic fashion. While it is recognized that the
monetary rewards in terms of bonus payments resulting from such a system may not



30
initially compare favorably to other sources of additional income, the system
would allow the WSCs' performance to be compared on a common basis providing
strong psychological motivation for management to improve company performance.
However, the introduction is intended to be selective, based on a judgement of
when such arrangements would yield positive results without becoming
bureaucratized.
4.21        Management incentives to improve performance via consumer involvement
are proposed to be institutionalized through the introduction of public
performance audits (PPAs) of the WSCs by an independent agency. Public disclosure
of comparative information relevant for users would enable consumer voice and
benchmarking competition to be used as effective tools in improving WSC
performance. PPAs are being introduced in the Philippines with Bank assistance.
4.22        Given the decision to reduce sectoral coordination at the national
government level, the Association of Water Supply Companies (AWSC) is currently
being strengthened to share experiences and best practices among the WSCs. For
example, the Danang WSC management has succeeded in universal metering and in
getting NRW down to 33% while the Haiphong WSC has implemented a system of
community involvement in managing water at the neighborhood level which has
decreased NRW from over 60% to below 40% in rehabilitated wards. This lends
confidence to the expectation that the potential exists to reduce NRW quickly and
relatively inexpensively in all WSCs. A workshop on commercial management of
water supply companies in a market economy has been requested by the AWSC and is
planned for August 1997 in collaboration with EDI.
4.23        Sector dialogue with the Government is continuing on gradual private
sector participation in the operations of the WSCs. Evaluation of the experience
gained during the implementation of the contract between Hanoi WBC and SI and BOT
schemes for bulk water provision would be used to propose suitable modifications
and extensions.



31
V. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
A. Rate Structure
5.1         Country-wide   water   tariffs   for   residential   customers,   state
organizations and businesses were maintained up to 1986 at the nominal rate of 1
VND/m3. Since then, they have been increased significantly in real terms, following
the Government's decision to charge users instead of covering all costs through
general taxes'.   In the early 1990s, the tasks of supplying water and setting
tariffs were decentralized, and provincial WSCs were created that were expected to
progressively become financially self-sufficient. Since then, the WSCs have
submitted every year tariff proposals, including an analysis of customer
affordability, to the PCs for approval. Currently, most customers are charged on
the basis of metered consumption; non-metered residential customers are charged on
assumed monthly consumption (usually 4 m3 per capita) and industrial-commercial
customers through negotiated contracts.
5.2         From 1990-1995, tariffs in the project cities have increased unevenly.
The average tariff in Quang Ninh is the highest, VND 1,800/m3 in 1995, compared to
VND 1,420/m3 in Hanoi, VND 1,150/m3 in Haiphong and VND 838/m3 in Danang.
Typically, domestic consumers pay 20% less than the average charge per m3, while
commercial consumers pay about 45% above the average.   Each city has several
categories (domestic, commercial and tourist facilities), with separate rates. In
Hanoi and Danang, tariffs include a 10% surcharge for sewerage. About 1,000 public
taps operate in Hanoi, and 650 in Haiphong. These provide free water in low-income
areas, but will be gradually phased out. All four WSCs have received approvals from
respective PCs for increased tariffs in 1996 at or above the rate used in the
financial projections (projections use 1995 as the base year as it is the last full
year for which complete accounts are available) for the IDA financed project.
5.3         A national water tariff policy study financed by the ADB was completed
in March 1996 and its recommendations are presently being reviewed by the
Government. The appraisal mission is in agreement with these recommendations which
also address issues of consumer protection and universal access. They include a
minimum monthly charge (covering 5 m3 for domestic and 10 m3 for non-domestic
customers), a lifeline block2 (varying between 12 and 20 m3/month depending on city
size set at a minimum of 1,500 VND/m3), a progressive tariff with three tariff
bands, and a separate tariff for industrial and commercial customers. The
recommendations also include universal metering, amortization of connection costs
through the monthly tariff and groundwater licensing. At negotiations assurances
were obtained from the Government that necessary actions would be taken through PCs,
including adjustment of water tariffs, to achieve required financial objectives of
Hanoi WBC and Haiphong, Danang and Quang Ninh WSCs.
I For the Hanoi WBC, domestic tariff increased 50 times over the period 1988-1995, starting from 20
VND/m3. During the same period, price of electricity to the company increased 12 times while average company
salary increased 14 times.
2 Specific problem related to apartment blocks equipped with one water meter is taken into consideration.



32
5.4         The study estimates the average incremental financial cost (as a proxy
for long run marginal cost) in Hanoi to be VND 2,867/m3 and concludes, based on an
affordability analysis, that tariffs cannot be raised to this level immediately but
would have to converge to the full cost over a period of more than five years. The
study recommends an average 1997 tariff in Hanoi of VND l,910/m3 which is considered
affordable based on an accepted 4% of household income affordability criterion. The
average tariff implemented in Hanoi in mid-1996 is VND 1,700/m3 which is in line
with the study recommendations.
B. Accounting and Auditing
5.5         The Ministry of Finance (MF) is responsible for issuing guidelines and
standards on the accounting and financial management of state organizations. WSCs
budget requests and reports are submitted through the TUPWS or CD to the PCs. The
finance department of each WSC uses cash-based accounting; it prepares monthly or
quarterly accounts using the recently introduced national standard accounting system
which supports cost-centered analysis of expenditures.   Year-end accounts are
currently audited by the PCs, but MF requires that independent auditors be appointed
in all cities during 1997. These auditors will have to be confirmed to be acceptable
to IDA. The WSCs will maintain the project accounts.
5.6         Hanoi WBC accounts were partly computerized from 1993-1995, and are
organized into internal cost accounting units covering the treatment plants and
distribution enterprises. The program to complete the computerization and transfer
existing accounts to the new system is underway.   The computerized billing and
collection system will be reviewed under the project and upgraded where necessary
to include control accounts.
5.7         Haiphong WSC manually prepared cash-based accounts through to 1995.  In
1996, under a Finnish-funded water supply and sanitation program, accounts are being
computerized and a management accounting and information system introduced to
support institutional development and help the managers effectively run the company.
A new computerized billing and collection and accounts receivable system will be
introduced under the project.
5.8         Quang Ninh WSC uses a manual general ledger system of accounts, with
transactions recorded on a cash basis. The project's technical assistance program
will upgrade the accounting and management information system and transfer manual
accounts to a computerized accrual-based system that uses an internal cost and
profit center analysis. It will begin in 1997 and be completed in 2-3 years.
5.9         Danang WSC's accounts are fully computerized using data base software.
Under the project, a technical assistance program would review the system to ensure
all accounting controls and internal cost accounting units are established, and that
results comply with internationally recognized accounting standards.
5.10        During negotiations, assurances were obtained that the annual financial
statements of the Hanoi WBC, Haiphong WSC, Quang Ninh WSC, and Danang WSC would be
audited by firms acceptable to the IDA and submitted to the IDA within nine months
of the close of each fiscal year. The Government would maintain the Statement of
Expenditures and the special accounts for the ITF credit and Danish Trust Fund, and
audit reports which would include separate opinions on these Statements, would be



33
prepared by firms acceptable to the IDA and submitted to the IDA within nine months
of the close of each fiscal year. It was agreed that because current accounting
systems and procedures are not capable of readily producing financial statements in
accordance with internationally accepted standards or principles and the fact that
the statements have not been audited in the past, the first audit reports would be
due by September 1999 and would include audits of the companies' accounts for FY98
and project accounts only for FY97.
C. Billing and Collections
5.11        In Hanoi, the WBC's district water enterprises read meters (25W of
registered connections are metered) and send the results to headquarters.   Monthly
bills are prepared centrally and include a 10% charge for sewerage. In 1995, there
were 128,000 registered customers, but partly due to administrative weaknesses, only
about 90,000 bills were issued each month. Payment is due within one month of the
billing. Accounts receivable at the end of 1995 were equal to 68 days' sales. The
bad debt provision for 1995 was 2% of gross sales, and the same figure was included
in financial projections through 2004.
5.12        About 44% of Haiphong's WSC connections are unmetered. The billing
system is computerized and uses data-base software, but does not include accounting
controls or a ledger.   Customers are given 10 days to pay,  and service is
disconnected after 20 days of non-payment. A Japanese Grant-financed technical
assistance to be implemented by the Haiphong WSC in 1997/1998 will design and
introduce a new computerized accounting software module for the billing, collection
and accounts receivable ledger. The new system will have adequate controls and will
be designed to interface with the general ledger.
5.13        Although over half the connections in Quang Ninh WSC are un-metered,
meter readers visit every connection once a month and manually prepare the bills and
collect the fees based on the meter data or number of occupants. The WSC recognizes
the system is unsatisfactory and needs to computerize the system.   Thus, the
technical assistance under the project will make this issue a priority.
5.14        All of Danang WSC's connections are metered and read every month and
customers are given 20 days to pay their bills. The billing system is computerized,
using data base software, but does not include proper accounting controls.  The
system will be reviewed and upgraded through the technical assistance program
provided under the project.
D. Fixed Assets and Revaluation
5.15        Until early 1995, fixed assets held by WSCs were owned by the PCs and
the depreciation charge was transferred partly or totally to the PCs, depending on
their share of investment funding.   Thus,  the WSCs were not responsible for
financing major replacements or new investments. To ensure that WSCs would self-
finance capital works and meet their debt service obligations, the Government
decided in 1995 to abolish the remittance of depreciation charges.
5.16        WSCs maintain registers for assets used in water operations, including
depreciation, over the estimated life expectancy. All the WSCs updated inventories
of fixed assets as of December 31, 1995, and these valuations have been used to



34
adjust financial projections.   The book value of fixed assets is projected to
increase each year by the annual inflation rate.
E. Past Financial Performance
5.17        The WSCs' recent financial performance in the project cities has been
unsatisfactory. In some, the companies appeared to cover a number of recurrent or
capital costs, but depreciation was considerably understated. Also, maintenance was
inadequate, which meant that true operating costs were not reflected on the income
statements.    Further,  the  PCs had  continually  subsidized  the  WSCs  for new
construction and major repairs, even as the water supply systems deteriorated and
water losses increased.   Since the end of 1994 programs for corrective actions
leading to elimination of subsidies have been introduced in Hanoi and Haiphong and
evidence from Haiphong indicates improving tendencies.

39
G. Financial Rates of Return
5.34        Based on the above financial projections presented in detail in Annex
9 and the assumptions presented in Annex 10, the financial internal rates of return
(FIRR) for the four project components have been calculated. The FIRRs for the
Hanoi, Haiphong, Quang Ninh and Danang components are 28%, 13%, 8% and 8%,
respectively. The analysis is presented in Annex 12.
5.35         The base year is taken as 1995, the last year for which actual data is
available. Without the project, operating efficiency would deteriorate significantly
due to ageing of already obsolete equipment. While this would be non-linear and
discontinuous, a linear approximation of a half percent real annual increase in
operating costs is used in the analysis to reflect all the impacts of the
deterioration. The 1995 tariff is assumed to remain constant in real terms since
there would be no justification for tariff increases without the project. Even if
tariffs are increased, real revenues are likely to remain unchanged because
consumers would disconnect from the utility and resort to self-provisioning in the
face of deteriorating service. In the "with project" scenario, O&M expenditures,
tariffs and percentage of water billed are assumed to increase as indicated in Annex
10. Efficiency gains that would result from lower per unit electricity consumption
are ignored. The FIRRs are calculated over 20 years and a 50% salvage value is
assumed.
5.36        The FIRRs are dependant on the projected tariff and NRW estimates which
have therefore been forecast conservatively. In Hanoi, for example, average tariff
per m3 is projected to increase from VND 1,420 in 1995 gradually to VND 2,213 in
1999, VND 3,331 in 2002 and VND 4,796 in 2004 yielding a total increase of 238% over
the period 1995-2004. The projected tariff stream is below the affordable tariff
stream recommended for Hanoi by the ADB National Tariff Study and has been agreed
by the Hanoi PC. The proposed tariff increase needs to be seen in reference to a
domestic tariff increase in Hanoi of 4900% during the period 1988-1995 (para 5.1)
5.37        NRW in Hanoi, for example, is projected to decrease from 69% in 1995 to
51% by 1999, 48% by 2002 and 46% by 2004. Given the findings of the customer
management survey conducted in Hanoi by Saur International (para 4.14), the decrease
of NRW in rehabilitated wards in Haiphong from 70% to 30* within three years and an
existing NRW of 33% in Danang, these targets are considered very reasonable.
However, continuing attention would need to be devoted to these parameters because
of the sensitivity of the FIRR to variations in them.
5.38        The sensitivity analysis reported in Annex 12 (Table A 12.5) shows that
the FIRR for the project falls from 16% to 13% if the tariff increase falls 10%
below forecasted values; to 15t if NRW reduction is 10% less than estimated; and to
14.5% if there is a 10% over run in capital costs. The FIRRs are insensitive to
project life and salvage value.
5.39        In standard analysis, the impact of tariff increases and metering on
consumption would need to be considered because of the price elasticity of demand.
However, standard analysis assumes no pent up demand, no change in the quality of
the product and no hidden expenditures asides from the price charged. None of these
assumptions holds in the case of the situation in Vietnam. The national tariff study
confirms that households are consuming less than their desired amount because of



40
supply constraints. Secondly, the water to be provided after the project would be
qualitatively different compared to that available presently both in terms of
quality and service level; willingness to pay for potable water at a higher service
level is greater than existing charges. Thirdly, households are spending to improve
the quality of existing water (through boiling, installing storage tanks and
operating electric pumps) an amount on average at least equal to if not exceeding
the existing monthly bill. Thus, the real cost of water is actually expected to
decrease after the project when the necessity for additional private expenditures
by households is removed. In addition to the above factors, urban incomes have been
rising steadily in Vietnam contributing to increased consumption demand. Given the
above, it is not considered necessary to adjust per capita consumption downwards
because of the projected increase in tariffs.
5.40        In relative terms, the rates of return vary substantially in the four
cities, which can be explained by city-specific conditions. It is highest in Hanoi
because both water production and efficiency increase significantly, it is the only
city with groundwater supply resulting in low production costs, and the customer
base is the largest, which brings high savings to consumers. In Haiphong, while the
project would not produce additional water, efficiency gains would be significant,
while project costs would be low since no new facilities would be built. Both Quang
Ninh and Danang have very low coverage (1995 population coverage in Quang Ninh and
Danang was 16% and 42%, respectively, compared to 82% and 98% in Hanoi and Haiphong)
resulting in higher per unit costs. In Danang, system efficiency (in terms of the
proportion of water that is billed) is already very high and improvement under the
project would be slight. However, project costs are the highest because completely
new intake and treatment facilities need to be constructed.
H. Monitoring and Evaluation
5.41        Performance would be monitored against the implementation schedule
(Annex 4) and the project implementation plan (Annex 5), while the agencies'
operational, financial and managerial performance will be assessed against the
monitoring indicators in Annex 11. The key performance indicators are percentage of
NRW, number of household connections, and the financial working ratio. These relate,
respectively, to the desired project goals of rehabilitating the networks, expanding
coverage, and improving financial performance of the WSCs. During negotiations,
assurances were obtained that the WSCs would provide IDA on June 30 and December 31
with semi-annual progress reports, including revised project cost estimates,
implementing schedules and monitoring indicators, the first of such reports to be
furnished by June 30, 1998. Annual updated financial projections would be submitted
no later than September 30 of each project year beginning in 1998.



41
VI. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
A. Proiect Benefits
6.1         The proposed project in Hanoi, Haiphong, Quang Ninh and Danang aims to:
(a) rehabilitate and expand facilities to meet coverage, consumption and quality
targets for the year 2000; (b) improve institutional capacity and operational
efficiency of the WSCs to sustain the physical investments; and (c) prepare an
investment and institutional development program to satisfy system demands after
2000 with maximum self-financing and private sector participation.
6.2         The  project  is  part  of  the  Government's  plan  to  upgrade  all
infrastructure in three key economic regions in order to remove constraints to
industrial development and tourism, to meet demands due to increased urbanization
and rising incomes, to meet environmental standards and to provide access to safe
drinking water. Hanoi, Haiphong and Quang Ninh are part of the northeastern economic
triangle and Danang is the designated growth center in central Vietnam.   Hanoi,
Haiphong and Danang have been identified for priority development as industrial and
commercial centers to absorb foreign direct investment while Danang and Halong City
in Quang Ninh province are designated for development as major tourist attractions.
6.3         Project contributions to development of industrial,  commercial and
tourism potential in the cities would translate into benefits resulting in higher
urban incomes and property values. Better service levels in terms of system
reliability, continuous supply and higher pressure would eliminate household
expenditures on storage tanks and electric motors designed to compensate for poor
service. Improved quality of drinking water which presently does not meet national
standards would eliminate household expenditures incurred on boiling water which is
a necessity in Vietnam. In addition, improved water quality would reduce health
expenditures resulting from the prevalence of water borne diseases. Households
presently without access to piped water would realize time and money savings when
recourse to vendors or wells would be made unnecessary in addition to health
benefits from the availability of potable water. Industrial and commercial
establishments currently pumping groundwater would not need to do so resulting in
environmental benefits.
6.4         Institutional development aimed at commercializing the water companies
would attract private investment in the sector and increase the ability of the
companies to self-finance future capital expenditure. Both outcomes would reduce the
need for public subsidies which could yield higher social benefits in other sectors.
6.5         Without the project, the deterioration of physical assets which have
suffered from prolonged lack of maintenance would accelerate. Equipment life would
be reduced, efficiency would decline, operating costs would increase, the quality
of water would deteriorate, more outages would occur and more consumers would
disconnect from the system, providing water for themselves. Water scarcities would
result given the increasing urban population. The possibility of water riots cannot
be ruled out; such riots directed against the water company occurred in Haiphong in
1993 resulting in the death of a company employee. The Government's infrastructure
package for the key economic regions would remain incomplete and ability of the
cities to attract foreign investment and tourism would be negatively affected.
Construction of facilities for tourists in a number of cities is already on hold



42
because of water and power shortages.
B. Least-Cost Solution
6.6         The Government's commitment to the project is not explicitly based on
a project rate of return analysis because a number of strategic social, development,
environmental and health objectives cannot be quantified precisely in terms of
benefits. There is no alternative to meeting, at the very least, the minimum
consumption requirements of the urban population, preferably with an acceptable
quality of water. Consequently, the Government's objective is to attain a minimum
defined level of coverage, consumption, service, and water quality by 2000 in the
major urban centers at the least cost. The alternative options evaluated are
detailed in the feasibility studies which are available in the project file. Out of
these, the least cost alternatives have been selected for the project.
C. Rate of Return
6.7         Notwithstanding the above, based on partial quantification of benefits
and costs and conservative assumptions, economic rates of return have been
calculated for the project components. The financial internal rates of return,
reported in para 5.34, are used as the starting point to compute the economic
internal rates of return.
6.8         To convert financial costs to economic costs, taxes and duties have been
eliminated. The relatively free exchange rate implies that there is no significant
foreign exchange premium. The major operating cost is due to electricity, comprising
more than half of total operating costs, which is being charged to the WSCs at the
long run marginal cost of electricity without any distortion. Regarding local wage
costs, it has been determined that market (or financial) wage rates broadly reflect
the opportunity cost of skilled labor in Vietnam taking into account the increasing
shortage of qualified technical staff. Although minimum wage levels may distort wage
rates for unskilled labor somewhat above market levels, the more skilled labor force
which predominates in the WSCs is likely to be virtually unaffected. With respect
to unskilled labor, a shadow wage factor of 0.6 is proposed. However, sensitivity
analysis indicates that the impact is insignificant since labor costs are a small
proportion of total supply costs. The prices of treatment chemicals are reported to
be distorted but again this is a small proportion and is ignored. Rather, the
impacts of labor and chemical cost distortions are captured through sensitivity of
the results to total costs. Net present value is calculated using a real discount
rate of 10%.
6.9         The only benefits quantified for inclusion in the economic analysis
relate to the coping costs consumers would avoid because of the project. Earlier
research has shown that water tariff is not the only cost borne by consumers.
Rather, the inefficiencies of water supply companies are passed on in terms of extra
costs to consumers who attempt to improve their service by installing underground
and overhead storage tanks and operate electric motors to pump water or use backup
devices like handpumps. Also, they incur additional expenses to purify the water



43
by boiling or using filters'. Data from HCM City indicates that households connected
to the water supply system incur additional costs of 300,000 VND per month.
Households without access to the piped system incur even higher costs since the
price of water purchased from vendors exceeds the price of piped water.
6.10        Full benefits of avoided coping costs are unlikely to be realized
immediately since they are dependent on changes in consumer behavior and confidence
in the reliability of the improved service which will build up gradually. In the
"with project" scenario, it is assumed that household avoided costs of 33,000 VND
per month would start being realized in 1999 and would stabilize at 45,000 VND per
month in 2001.
6.11        Benefits resulting from improved health and environment, higher incomes
and property values due to the development impact of improved water service, and
availability of piped water to non-domestic users presently relying on groundwater
are ignored in the analysis. Therefore, the rates of return calculated will be
grossly under-estimated and are only intended to confirm that they are above the
hurdle rate commonly used for water supply projects.
6.12        In the "without project" scenario, the continued system deterioration
would impose increased coping costs on domestic consumers. It is conservatively
assumed that additional monthly coping costs of 2,000 VND would be imposed. These
would increase by 2,000 VND per year stabilizing at a level of 10,000 VND per month
after 5 years. Costs to non-domestic consumers and to the economy resulting from
infrastructure bottlenecks are ignored.
6.13        Resource costs that merit consideration in a water supply project
pertain to the cost of the raw water and environmental costs. In Hanoi, the
groundwater aquifer is recharged from the Red River and is not mined. The reserves
are estimated to be sufficient to meet demand upto 2010. There is no alternative use
of the groundwater and therefore no opportunity cost is attributed. In Quang Ninh
province, water is provided from a dedicated dam with no agricultural use and
involving no opportunity cost. In Haiphong and Danang, surface water is used which
could have alternative employment in agriculture. An opportunity cost of 50 VND/m3
is used based on data available from the National Tariff Study.
6.14        Environmental costs pertain to the cost of disposing the wastewater
generated. However, in the proposed project, net additional water is not being
produced. First, capacity expansion is limited. Second, the additional pped water
produced by the WSCs would provide access to presently non-connected households.
These households are presently using water from alternative sources which would be
substituted by the piped water. Given that the project aims to provide 80% coverage
at minimum consumption levels, the entire impact would be one of substitution,
displacing non-piped sources. Therefore environmental costs are not imputed to the
project.
6.15        Based  on  the  above  set  of  conservative  assumptions  and  partial
1 The 1992 World Development Report, pp. 98-99, estimates that in Jakarta, Indonesia, households spend
an amount equal to one percent of the city's gross domestic product, US$50 million, only on boiling water.



44
quantification of benefits and costs, the estimates of the economic internal rates
of return (EIRR) for the four project components and the overall project are as
follows: Hanoi, 19%; Haiphong, 24%; Quang Ninh, 12%; Danang, 11%; and the overall
project, 17%. All rates exceed the commonly used hurdle rate of 10t and confirm that
the project as a whole and each component individually is economically justified.
6.16        Actual rates of return based on inclusion of all benefits are likely to
be an order of magnitude higher than those computed and therefore quite robust
against typical variations in costs and benefits. Benefits, in terms of avoided
costs, have been estimated very conservatively. However, we can test project
sensitivity to a further 10% decrease in benefits. Typical cost overruns are not
expected to exceed lot of capital and O&M costs. Capital costs are designed to be
controlled because of procurement through single supply and install contracts in
each city. O&M costs have a lot of slack built in them because existing costs are
not adjusted for efficiencies resulting from reduced electricity consumption because
of replacement of ageing and obsolete equipment and from reduction in staff levels
as the WSCs are commercialized.
6.17        Sensitivity analysis shows that the overall project rate of return falls
from 17% to 12% for the worst case, where benefits decrease by 10% and both capital
and O&M costs increase by 10%. For 10% percent variations from the base case, the
rate of return is most sensitive to benefit reduction (falling to 14%), less
sensitive to capital cost increases (falling to 15%) and least sensitive to an
increase in O&M costs (falling to 16%). In no situation does the project EIRR fall
below acceptable levels. Annex 12 presents the assumptions made in calculating the
economic rates of return, the data used and the results including the sensitivity
analysis.
6.18        An alternative approach to quantifying project benefits would be to use
estimates of consumer willingness to pay per m3 of improved water. Studies in other
countries have consistently found that average willingness-to-pay for better service
exceeds the existing tariff charged. According to the survey carried out for the
National Water Tariff Policy Study (para 5.3), this is also true in Vietnam, where
the provision of water has been considered a social obligation and tariffs have been
considerably subsidized. However, methodologically acceptable estimates of consumer
willingness to pay for improved water are not available for the cities covered by
the proposed project.
D. Affordability
6.19        Because of the avoided coping costs detailed above, the total price of
household water consumption is expected to fall after the project is implemented.
However, affordability analyses available for most developing countries exclude
coping costs and examine only the burden resulting from the price of piped water.
Surveys conducted for the National Water Tariff Policy Study show that existing
tariffs are affordable and that higher tariffs proposed by the Study could be
charged in 1995 without exceeding 4 percent of household income for ordinary and
"lifeline" domestic consumers. The 4 percent rule is commonly used as a measure of
affordability for water supply.
6.20        Hanoi was the only city covered by the Study which is also included in
the proposed project. Average monthly incomes for all households and those in the



45
lowest quintile were estimated at 1,200,000 VND and 650,000 VND, respectively, while
the corresponding expenditures for water were 19,500 VND and 11,300 VND.  These
expenditures translate into 1.6% and 1.5% of household income, well below the 4%
threshold.   The percentages were 2.6% and 2.8%, respectively in Thanh Hoa, the
smallest and poorest urban center included in the survey.
6.21        Based on the results and affordability analysis,  the Tariff Study
proposed an average tariff for Hanoi of 3560 VND per cubic meter in 2000 (the
average tariff in 1995 was 1420 VND) . By comparison, the financial projections for
the proposed IDA project are based on a tariff of 2478 VND in the same year. Thus,
the tariffs agreed to by the provincial governments are within the commonly accepted
affordability threshold. Reduction of coping costs would further decrease the total
costs presently being borne by households in the project cities.
E. Project Risks
6.22        Since the physical components  of the project deal primarily with
rehabilitating existing facilities, there are no unusual risks.  No irreversible
environmental impacts are expected and the protection of water sources and options
for disposal of treatment sludge (identified during project preparation) will be
addressed through special studies during implementation.
6.23        The engineering preparation was guided by the need to reduce land
acquisition and resettlement as much as possible. Thus, the project will require
only limited land acquisition, relocation of population and demolition of houses
because most of the treatment plants, intakes and wellfields are located away from
residential areas.
6.24        A major risk to the project could stem from inadequate maintenance of
the rehabilitated facilities. Therefore, as part of project preparation, the water
companies have conducted an asset inventory which is being used to produce
maintenance guidelines based on standard engineering codes of practice. These
guidelines would be used to ensure adequate budgetary allocation for prevention and
maintenance.
6.25        Another risk to project sustainability could arise from inadequate
incentives to improve financial and managerial performance. Therefore, the project
proposes a number of institutional development measures to be undertaken before and
during project implementation. These include training field and office personnel,
improving the billing, collection and customer management systems, introducing
performance contracts between the provincial governments and the water supply
companies, and public performance audits of all water supply companies by an
independent agency. Collaboration with a reputable international water systems
operator and with EDI has been initiated to assist with some of these tasks which
are considered critical success factors for the project.
6.26        Shortage of counterpart funds and coordination of activities funded by
different donors could slow project implementation. Financial plans indicating the
level of funding required from the provincial governments have been discussed and
agreed with the PCs and have been made part of the terms under which project
components have been approved by the Office of Government. Coordination problems are
mitigated by substantial assistance from the Resident Mission.



46
VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
7.1         At negotiations, assurances were obtained from the Government that:
a.    The Government would provide local funds as required by WSCs for
carrying out of their parts of the project (para 3.20);
b.    The Government would cause the PCs to take all actions to enable the
respective WSCs to implement the project and comply with their obligations (para
4.2);
c.    The Government would take necessary actions, including adjustment of
water tariffs, to achieve required financial objectives of Hanoi WBC and Haiphong,
Danang and Quang Ninh WSCs (para 5.3); and
d.    The Government would maintain the Statement of Expenditures and the
special accounts for the ITF credit, and audit reports, which would include separate
opinions on these Statements, would be prepared by firms acceptable to the IDA and
submitted to the IDA within nine months of the close of each fiscal year (para
5.10)-
7.2         At negotiations, assurances were obtained from the WSCs that:
a.    The WSCs would submit each year, beginning October 30, 1997, annual
plans for institutional assistance and training to IDA for review (para 3.23);
b.    The WSCs would implement their respective RAPs and would arrange for:
(i) semiannual progress reports on WSCs' supervision and monitoring of resettlement
activities; (ii) starting from December 31, 1997, an independent annual monitoring
of RAPs' implementation; and (iii) final report on completion of each RAP (para
3.28) . IDA approval of a detailed (distribution) RAP in respect of each network
site would be required prior to commencement of works on that part of the project.
These would be prepared by WSCs and approved by PCs (para 3.28);
c.    The  Quang Ninh WSC  would  furnish by December  31,  1997  proposed
arrangements for periodic inspections of the Cao Van dam and that it would implement
recommendations of such inspections thereafter (para 3.33);
d.    The annual financial statements of the Hanoi WBC, Haiphong WSC, Quang
Ninh WSC, and Danang WSC would be audited by firms acceptable to the IDA and
submitted to the IDA within nine months of the close of each fiscal year. The first
audit reports would be due by September 1999 and would include audits of the
companies' accounts for FY98 and project accounts only for FY97. (para 5.10);
e.    Hanoi  and  Haiphong  WSCs  would  take  necessary  measures  including
adjusting the structure or level of tariffs, to ensure they recover operating costs
in full as well as maintain a debt service coverage ratio of at least 1.2, and a
self-financing ratio of at least 3W beginning FY98, and 20% in FY02 (paras 5.20 and
5.24);
f.    Quang Ninh and Danang WSCs would take necessary measures including
adjusting the structure or level of tariffs, to maintain for each of their fiscal



47
years a ratio of total expenses to total operating revenues not higher than 100t in
FY98, 85% beginning in FY02, and 65% in FY05 (paras 5.28 and 5.33); and
h.    The WSCs would provide IDA with semiannual progress reports including
revised project cost estimates, implementing schedules and monitoring indicators on
June 30 and December 31 each year, the first of such reports to be furnished by June
30, 1998. Annual updated financial projections would be submitted no later than
September 30 of each project year, beginning in 1998 (para 5.41).
7.3         At negotiations, the Government and WSCs confirmed (a) onlending terms
(para 3.20); (b) adherence to implementation action plans (para 3.24); (c) adherence
to environmental management plans  (para 3.49);  (d)  adherence  to action plan
regarding obligations pertaining to households resettled from the Cao Van dam (para
3.30); (e) the content of periodical reports (para 3.52); and (f) their financial
plans (paras 5.18, 5.22, 5.26, 5.30).
7.4         The execution of Australian, Danish and Finnish Grant Agreements and
Danish Trust Fund (para 3.20) and the establishment of Resettlement Committees by
each of the PCs (para 3.28) are conditions of effectiveness.
7.5         Execution of subsidiary loan agreements between the Government and Hanoi
WBC, and Haiphong, Quang Ninh and Danang WSCs (para 3.20), and employment of
construction management consultants acceptable to IDA is a condition of disbursement
of funds related to that part of the project (para 3.22). Full disbursement of the
proceeds of the Danish Trust Fund is a condition of disbursement of the ITF credit
earmarked for the respective project component (para 3.40). Compliance with the
obligation to provide some additional assets (land and electrical connections) to
households resettled in Gia Lai province is a condition of disbursement of the
proceeds of the credit earmarked to finance all of Quang Ninh WSC project components
(para 3.30).
7.6         Subject to the above assurances and conditions, the proposed project
constitutes a suitable basis for an ITF credit of SDR 71.3 million (US$98.6 million
equivalent) , for a period of 40 years, including a grace period of ten years, at IDA
standard terms. The borrower would be the Socialist Republic of Vietnam.

48                     ANNEX  1
VIETNAM
WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Existing water supply, sewerage and solid waste services
HANOI COMPONENT
Population
1.    Hanoi, the country's capital, is Vietnam's second largest city, one of four
with provincial status. It is situated in the low-income and less developed
northern part of Vietnam, in the Red River delta some 100 km from the sea and
Haiphong port. In 1994 it accounted for about 6% of the country's GNP mainly made
up of services such as government, commerce, banking and education but also
manufacturing and agriculture. Nearly 900,000 are employed; 30% in services and
the remainder equally distributed between manufacturing and agriculture.
2.    The city is divided into four urban districts (quan) namely Ba Dinh, Hoan
Kiem, Dong Da and Hai Ba Trung, and five sub-urban districts ( Tu Liem, Gia Lam,
Than Tri, Dong Anh and Me Linh). The districts are divided into wards (phuong)
each containing 10,000-15,000 population. Since the city has provincial status
its Peoples' Committee reports directly to the national government and the
structure of the city government generally reflects that of the national
government. Typical organization of provincial government is shown in Chart 2.
3.    Hanoi's population has grown from 1.8 million in 1985 to about 2.2 million
in 1995, and is expected to increase to 2.5 million in 2005. The administrative
area of Hanoi city is 925 km2, of which about 883 km2 form a rural belt of five
suburban districts surrounding the developed urban center of about 42 km2. The
population of the four urban districts has grown from 0.8 million in 1985 to 1.1
million in 1995 and is expected to grow to 1.2 million in 2005. Population
density in urban Hanoi varies from about 80 persons per hectare (p/ha) to 1,400
p/ha with the most densely populated parts of the city in the old center (Hoan
Kiem district) and some parts of neighboring Hai Ba Trung and Dong Da districts.
4.    Urban land use master plan of 1993 proposed that by 2010 about 110 ha of
land currently used by agriculture be urbanized. Industrial areas are planned to
be relocated in suburban fringe downstream of the city where large area of land
will be prepared. Small scale industries will still remain in the urban area.
Water supply
5.    The water supply services are managed by Hanoi Water Business Company
(WBC),  a provincial business enterprise1,  reporting to the city's People's
' A business enterprise performs an economic activity and generates revenues from its sales. It is expected
to be financially self-supporting and legally independent.



49                     ANNEX  1
Committee through its Transport and Urban Public Works Service (TUPWS). In the
current form, WBC was established in April 1994 through merging the earlier water
company, the water investment company and the training center. WBC staff of about
1,660 is divided into four functional groupings: (a) production, headed by the
deputy director, with the responsibility for water production in the eight water
plants, the electro-mechanical enterprise and the transport enterprise; (b)
distribution, headed by the deputy director, with the responsibility for water
distribution through six water business enterprises, the meter reading, billing,
collection and installation enterprise, and materials enterprise, which provides
stores support for the entire company; (c) services departments, which include
departments for planning, finance, organization and training, and legal, all
reporting directly to the general director and (d) construction.
6.    The production and distribution capacity of the WBC piped system in 1995
was 353,000 m3/d and the daily average production was 320,000 m3/d, including
about 69% of non-revenue water. Water services were provided to about 900,000
consisting of about 800,000 of the population in the four urban districts and
100,000 living in neighboring suburban districts through 123,000 connections and
about 1,000 public standposts. Out of total service connections, about 96% were
residential, using about 18% of water supplied; 49 were commercial, institutional
and industrial, using 13%; remaining 69 t was non-revenue water. Private wells,
both deep and shallow, are estimated to provide about 140,000 m3/d in addition
to WBC production, servicing additional about 110,000 people, commerce and
industry in WBC supply area.    The projected daily average water demand and
production capacity assuming 25% of NRW are shown in Table A-1.1.

7.    The water source is groundwater extracted by wells from alluvial deposits
along the Red River. The raw water has a high content of iron and manganese and
is treated prior to distribution. The waterworks facilities include 130 wells
65-70 m deep with diameters of 600 mm, clustered in 13 wellfields, eight



50                     ANNEX  1
treatment plants of sizes varying from 18,000 m3/d to 60,000 m3/d, nine balancing
reservoirs with the total volume of 11,200 m3, eight treated water pump stations
with the total capacity of 9,900 m3/hour (240,000 m3/d) and about 400 km of mains
with diameters varying from 60 mm to 600 mm. The tertiary distribution network
is practically non-existent.
8.    During last ten years the WBC system has been rehabilitated with help from
Finnish government. These works, expected to be completed in early 1997, include
improvement of production and distribution facilities. All the rehabilitated
treatment plants work satisfactorily and completed parts of the network display
substantial improvement in the water pressure, however, parts of the old town
with population of about 170,000 still experience very low pressure and
intermittent service and remain to be rehabilitated. Since Hanoi solely depends
on groundwater the ever growing extractions by the WBC and private wells resulted
in some areas in land subsidence, reaching 5-10 mm per annum.
Sewerage and drainaqe
9.    Sewerage and drainage services are managed by the Hanoi Sanitation and
Drainage Company (SDC), an administrative enterprise2, reporting to city's PC
through its TUPWS. In 1995 SDC maintained 120 km of sewers and 35 km of open
channels operating as combined sewerage system. The system serves about 30% of
the urban area, is overloaded and in poor state of repair; it discharges into
city's lakes and rivers without treatment and level of maintenance and cleaning
is low. TUPWS, assisted by the Government of Japan, is preparing improvement and
expansion of the system with construction planned to start in 1998.
Nightsoil and septage disposal
10.   It is estimated that Hanoi population generates about 40 t/d of nightsoil
from bucket collection, 60 t/d is stored in pit and vault latrines and 150 t/d
is disposed of in septic tanks. Of this quantity about 60-70 t/d of nightsoil and
septage from pits, vaults and septic tanks is collected and disposed of at Me Tri
landfill. The remaining is used by farmers.
Solid waste management
11.   The  solid  waste  services  are  managed  by  URENCO,  an  administrative
enterprise, reporting to PC through its TUPWS. In 1995 URENCO has collected daily
about 300 ton of solid waste and disposed it of at the Me Tri landfill about 8
km west from the city center. Only about 35%-40% of total generated solid waste
estimated at 1,000 t/d is collected. The rest is burned or buried at source or
scattered. The landfill has reached the end of its life and is being closed. A
new site at Tham Hiep with an area of about 20 ha and with expected life of 5
years has been selected and the construction started in 1996.
2 An administrative enterprise renders public service to the community. It is almost entirely funded from
public sources, normally from the budget of the local government by which it is licensed.



51                     ANNEX  1
HAIPHONG COMPONENT
Population
12.   Haiphong is Vietnam's fourth largest city, one of the four with provincial
status. It is situated in the less developed northern part of Vietnam, on the
right bank of the Red River delta in the Gulf of Tonkin. The major economic
activities include the port which is the biggest in Vietnam, the shipyard and
cement factory. Nearly 600,000 are employed; 30% in services and the remainder
equally distributed between manufacturing and agriculture.
13.   The city is divided in three urban districts (Hong Bang, Le Chan and Ngo
Quyen), and seven rural districts (Thuy Nguyen, An Hai, An Lao, Kien Thuy, Tien
Lang, Vin Bao and the island district Cat Hai). The semi-urban neighborhoods are
being developed in the south of the urban districts around towns Kien An and Do
So, about 8 km and 15 km distant, respectively. These are planned to be upgraded
to urban districts in the near future. Since the city has provincial status its
Peoples' Committee reports directly to the national government and the structure
of the city government generally reflects that of the national government.
14.   Haiphong's population of 1.6 million in 1993 is expected to grow to about
1.8 million in 2000, and to 2.2 million in 2010. The administrative area of
Haiphong city is 1,500 km2, of which about 1,480 km2 form a circle of islands and
rural districts around the developed urban center with and area of 20 km2,
planned to increase to 27 km2 and 37 km2 in 2000 and 2010. The 1993 population
in the three urban districts of 400,000 is expected to grow to 450,000 and
550,000 in 2000 and 2010. The 1993 population of Kien An and Do So towns of
88,000 is expected to grow to 100,000 and 120,000 in 2000 and 2010.
Water supply
15.   Water supply services are managed by Haiphong Water Supply Company (WSC),
a business enterprise, reporting to the city's PC through TUPWS. Haiphong WSC
service area includes three urban districts, Kien An and Do Son towns. Its staff
of 620 is divided into four functional groupings: (a) production, laboratory and
workshop, headed by the deputy director; (b) consumer services, distribution and
booster stations, also headed by the deputy director; (c) services departments,
which include planning, materials, personnel, accounting and administration all
reporting directly to the General Director; and (d) construction.
16.   The production and distribution capacity of the water supply system and the
daily average production in 1995 was 125,000 m3/d or 300 lcd, including about 69%
of non-revenue water. Water services were provided to commerce, institutions,
industry and 420,000 or 98% of urban population through 31,000 metered and 25,000
unmetered connections.   Out of 56,000 service connections,  about 97% were
residential, using about 24% of water supplied; 3% were commercial, institutional
and industrial, using 7% of water; remaining 69% was non-revenue water.  The
projected daily average water demand and production capacity assuming 25% of NRW
are shown in Table A-1.2.

17.   The source is surface water treated in five treatment plants. All plants
are supplied from tidal regulated rivers fed by Thai Binh River system flowing
to Haiphong from northwest. The raw water has occasionally high turbidity and
shows low levels of organic pollution. The waterworks facilities include five
intakes, several kilometers of raw water transmission canals and mains, five
treatment plants of sizes varying from 5,000 m3/d to 60,000 m3/d, six balancing
reservoirs with the total volume of 3,050 m3, five treated water pump stations
with the total capacity of 4,100 m3/hour (95,000 m3/d) and about 160 km of
transmission mains with diameters varying from 150 mm to 600 mm. Most
transmission mains are made of cast iron and galvanized steel. The tertiary
distribution network is practically non-existent. The majority of mains have
never been pressurized over one bar, external and internal corrosion is common,
and joints and fittings are leaking, generally beyond repair.
18.   The WSC system is rehabilitated with help from Finnish government. The
works  include  improvement  of production  and distribution  facilities.  The
reduction of non-revenue water has been implemented from 1994 by constructing new
"phuong distribution systems". Phuong system normally has no more than three
connections to secondary network and all houses are provided with metered
connections. The experiment started nearest to An Duong treatment plant and by
December 1995 nine phuongs with 50, 000 population were rehabilitated and provided
with 11,000 connections. Completed parts of the network display substantial
improvement in the water pressure. The majority of the town which remains to be
rehabilitated experience very low pressure and intermittent service.
Sewerage and drainage
19.   Sewerage and drainage services in the three urban districts are managed by
the Haiphong Sewerage and Drainage Company (SDC) an administrative enterprise
reporting to PC through TUPWS. Other towns, such as Kien An and Do Son, have



53                     ANNEX  1
their own organizations for environmental services. Urban Haiphong is served by
170 km of combined sewers conveying stormwater and wastewater. The network
consists of open ditches, close conduits and several major box sewers. Due to its
age and absence of maintenance the network is in a poor state of repair. Besides
on-site treatment, such as septic tanks or some basic facilities in industrial
establishments, no treatment of wastewater exists. Household sanitation is very
basic. The 1989 census showed that about 11,000 (46%) of urban household have no
latrine of their own, often shared with neighboring households. Remaining 54% of
households used 420 public latrines or resort to any other open place.
Nichtsoil and septage disposal
20.   About 35 t/d of nightsoil was collected in 1995 in urban Haiphong. In
addition, about 100 t/d is used for small individual gardening. Septage from
about 5,500 septic tanks and the nightsoil is either sold to farmers or disposed
of in the landfill and also in the nearest sewer or lake. The septage and
nightsoil are applied to agricultural land and fishpond with no preliminary
treatment or conditioning.
Solid waste management
21.   The solid waste services are managed by Haiphong URENCO, an administrative
enterprise established in 1964, reporting to PC through its TUPWS. In addition
to solid waste URENCO also manages collection and disposal of septage, nightsoil
and solid waste, cleans streets and operates public latrines. Daily generation
of solid waste was estimated in 1995 at 320 t/d, of which 250 t/d (440 m3/d) were
collected by URENCO and 70 t/d was left in the service area, mainly in the back
street and open spaces which are littered with garbage. A considerable portion
of waste is organic, recyclable materials such as paper, plastic, metal and glass
are normally scavenged before collection. The collected waste is disposed of in
a landfill dump at Thuong Ly (in Hong Bang district). Of the total area of 10 ha
about 7 ha have been filled up to elevation of 4.0 m above sea level.
OUANG NINH COMPONENT
Population
22.   The component includes Halong City and Cam Pha town, situated along the
scenic coast in the Quang Ninh province in the Northern Mountains Region of
Vietnam. With 1994 population of nearly 900,000 and an area of 5,900 km2 Quang
Ninh is of a medium size among Vietnam's 53 provinces. The province as well as
the component area are characterized by steep hills with residential locations
on the low-lands between. The components' 1994 total population was 278,000
living in 62,000 households.
23.   Halong City, the capital of the province, acquired city status in 1994
through merging the towns Hong Gai and Bai Chay, each located on one bank of the
Dien Vong River estuary. The industries and the administration are mostly in the
eastern part, Hong Gai, while tourist hotels are in the western part, Bai Chay.
The 1993 Master Plan projects the City population to grow from the current



54                     ANNEX  1
124,000 to 510,000 in 2010, mainly through absorbing neighboring districts
including Cam Pha, and its area to grow from 124 ha to 340 ha in 2010. Cam Pha
town, with 1994 population of 132,000, is a district capital 20 km east of Halong
City along the coastal road to the Chinese border 70 km distant. The district is
dominantly rural with most of the population concentrated around Cam Pha town.
24.   With about 95% of country's anthracite reserves in the province Halong City
and Cam Pha town developed into the nations' largest center of coal mining and
processing. Other industrial activities include shipbuilding, cement production,
fisheries and seafood processing. Shipping is to be greatly increased through a
new port for general cargo currently being developed in Dien Vong River estuary
while the coal port is being moved in Cam Pha. The total labor force was
estimated in 1994 at 115,000 of which more that 60% were employed in mining and
manufacturing and the rest in services and administration.
Water supply
25.   Water supply services are managed by the Quang Ninh Water Supply Company
(WSC), a provincial business enterprise, reporting to the Provincial PC through
its Construction Bureau. It was established in 1976 and re-registered in 1992.
WSC has 475 permanent employees headed by the Director assisted by two Vice-
Directors in charge of the Administration Department and the Technical
Department. Operations of the Water Company are decentralized into six water
supply enterprises3 and one mechanical workshop unit. Each enterprise is headed
by a director reporting to the Director of the Company and normally include an
administration department and several technical teams such as for operating
treatment plant, pumping stations or distribution network for water supply
enterprises or for a typical specialization in the workshop unit.
26.   The 1995 production and distribution capacity of the four water supply
enterprises included in the component was 51,600 m3/d and the daily average
production was 28,000 m3/d or 650 lcd, including about 56% of non-revenue water.
Water services were provided through 13,000 connections to commerce, industry and
43,200 population (15% of total) of which 9,500 were in Bai Chay, 28,200 in Hong
Gai and 5,500 in Cam Pha. About 97 e of all connections were residential, using
about 17% of water supplied and 3 % were commercial and industrial using 27% of
water supplied. Remaining 56% was non-revenue water.   Private shallow wells,
estimated to provide about 12,000 m3/d in addition to public supply, are polluted
and should be considered a temporary source. The projected daily average water
demand and production capacity assuming 25% of NRW are shown in Table A-1.3.
27.   The water originates from surface and ground sources. The surface source
serving Bai Chay is Dong Ho River; an intake and Dong Ho treatment plant with a
capacity of 20,000 m3/d are 10 km from Bai Chay. The source serving Hong Gai/Cam
Pha is Dien Vong River; an intake and Dien Vong treatment plant, with a capacity
3 Water enterprises are: Bai Chai, Hong Gai, Cam Pha, and Dien Vong treatment plant; last two, Uong Bi
and Mao Khe, are not included in the component.



55                     ANNEX  1
of 15,00 m3/d are about 20 km from Hong Gai. In both instances the facilities
include intakes formed by a weir across the river, raw water pumping and rising
main, treatment plant, treated water pumping and rising main. The ground water
is pumped from thirteen deep wells providing about 1,600 m3/d and 15,000 m3/d to
Bai Chay and Hong Gai/Cam Pha, respectively. The distribution network consists
of eighteen balancing reservoirs with the total volume of 22,300 m3, several
boosting pumping stations, and about 125 km of mains with diameters varying from
100-800 mm. The tertiary distribution network is practically non-existent. Its
functions have been replaced by extremely long individual connections (spaghetti
connections) which draw water directly from mains.
